The Ruling on Investigating the Soul
The author explains the prohibition of investigating the soul's essence and provides evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why delving into the essence of the soul (al-ruh) is considered disliked (makruh) and the proper methodological approach to matters of the unseen.

Core Idea
The soul's essence is a divine secret; inquiry without revelation is improper.

In this lesson, we examine the ruling on discussing the reality of the soul (al-ruh). The author presents two groups: those who refrained from speculation and those who engaged in it, arguing that the correct approach is to avoid delving into matters for which no revealed text exists, as the soul is among the unseen (mughayyabat) known only to Allah. We will read the source passage and unpack its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the full translation. Notice the author's progression from the poetic line to the two groups and the evidence.
The passage includes a Qur'anic verse and a quote from al-Junayd.
2
Identify the two groups
Find where the author describes the two groups. Note the first group's stance (refraining) and why it is preferred. Then note the second group's approach and the caution about it.
The preferred group follows the Salaf and Ibn Abbas.
3
Understand the ruling and evidence
Focus on the ruling: delving is disliked (makruh) because no text (nass) has come from the Lawgiver. Notice the citation of al-Junayd and the verse from Surah al-Isra. Reflect on the methodology: matters of the unseen are only known through revelation.
The author emphasizes proper conduct (adab) with the Lawgiver.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلا نَخُضْ فِي الرُّوحِ إِذْ مَا وَرَدَا نَصٌّ عَنِ الشَّارِعِ لَكِنْ وُجِدَا. وَلَمَّا اخْتَلَفَ النَّاسُ فِي الرُّوحِ أَيْضًا عَلَى فِرْقَتَيْنِ: فِرْقَةٌ أَمْسَكَتْ عَنِ الْكَلَامِ فِيهَا؛ لأَنَّهَا سِرٌّ مِنْ أَسْرَارِهِ تَعَالَى، لَمْ يُؤْتَ عِلْمَهُ الْبَشَرُ، وَكَانَتْ هَذِهِ الطَّرِيقَةُ هِيَ الْمُخْتَارَةَ؛ صَدَّرَ النَّاظِمُ جَازِمًا بِهَا فَقَالَ: (وَلا نَخُضْ) نَحْنُ مَعَاشِرَ جُمْهُورِ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ (فِي) بَيَانِ حَقِيقَةِ (الرُّوحِ) بِجِنْسٍ وَفَصْلٍ مُمَيَّزَيْنِ لَهَا؛ لِتَعَذُّرِ الْوُقُوفِ عَلَيْهِمَا؛ لِعَدَمِ وُرُودِ السَّمْعِ بِهِمَا، وَلا يُتَلَقَّيَانِ إِلَّا مِنْهُ. وَأَشَارَ إِلَى عِلَّةِ النَّهْيِ عَنِ الْخَوْضِ فِيهَا عَلَى هَذِهِ الطَّرِيقَةِ، بِأَنَّهُ خِلافُ الأَدَبِ مَعَ الشَّارِعِ حَيْثُ لَمْ يُبَيِّنْهَا لِنَبِيِّهِ. بِقَوْلِهِ: (إِذْ مَا وَرَدَا)، أَيْ: عَدَمُ خَوْضِنَا فِي بَيَانِهَا عَلَى سَبِيلِ النَّدْبِ، فَالْخَوْضُ فِي بَيَانِ حَقِيقَتِهَا مَكْرُوهٌ؛ لِعَدَمِ التَّوْقِيفِ فِي ذَلِكَ؛ إِذْ هِيَ مِنَ الْمُغَيَّبَاتِ الَّتِي لا تُعْرَفُ إِلَّا مِنْ قِبَلِ الشَّارِعِ، وَلَمْ يَرِدْ (نَصٌّ) أَيْ دَلِيلٌ (عَنِ الشَّارِعِ) - وَهُوَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى - بِبَيَانِهَا؛ لأَنَّ نَبِيَّنَا لَمْ يُبَلِّغْنَا ذَلِكَ عَنْهُ، وَكُلُّ مَا هُوَ كَذَلِكَ فَالأَوْلَى الْكَفُّ عَنِ الْخَوْضِ فِيهِ. وَلِذَا قَالَ الْجُنَيْدُ: الرُّوحُ شَيْءٌ اسْتَأْثَرَ اللَّهُ بِعِلْمِهِ، وَلَمْ يُطْلِعْ عَلَيْهِ أَحَدًا مِنْ خَلْقِهِ، فَلا يَجُوزُ لِعِبَادِهِ الْبَحْثُ عَنْهُ بِأَكْثَرَ مِنْ أَنَّهُ مَوْجُودٌ؛ قَالَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَيَسْأَلُونَكَ عَنِ الرُّوحِ قُلِ الرُّوحُ مِنْ أَمْرِ رَبِّي﴾ [الإسراء: 85]، أَيْ: مِمَّا اسْتَأْثَرَ اللَّهُ بِعِلْمِهِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِعَجْزِ الْمَرْءِ حَيْثُ لَمْ يَعْلَمْ حَقِيقَةَ نَفْسِهِ الَّتِي بَيْنَ جَنْبَيْهِ مَعَ الْقَطْعِ بِوُجُودِهِ، فَيَرُدُّ الْعِلْمَ إِلَيْهِ سُبْحَانَهُ مَعَ الإِقْرَارِ بِالْعَجْزِ عَنْ إِدْرَاكِ مَا لَمْ يُطْلِعْهُ اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ، وَعَلَى هَذِهِ الطَّرِيقَةِ ابْنُ عَبَّاسٍ وَالسَّلَفُ، وَيَجْرِي عَلَيْهَا الْوَقْفُ عَنِ الْجَزْمِ بِمَحَلٍّ مَخْصُوصٍ لَهُ مِنَ الْبَدَنِ. وَلَمْ يَخْرُجِ النَّبِيُّ مِنَ الدُّنْيَا حَتَّى أَطْلَعَهُ اللَّهُ عَلَى جَمِيعِ مَا أَبْهَمَهُ عَنْهُ؛ لَكِنَّهُ أَمَرَ بِكَتْمِ الْبَعْضِ وَالإِعْلامِ بِالْبَعْضِ الآخَرِ. وَالْفِرْقَةُ الثَّانِيَةُ تَكَلَّمَتْ فِيهَا؛ وَبَحَثَتْ عَنْ حَقِيقَتِهَا، قَالَ النَّوَوِيُّ: وَأَصَحُّ مَا قِيلَ فِيهَا عَلَى هَذِهِ الطَّرِيقَةِ، مَا قَالَهُ إِمَامُ الْحَرَمَيْنِ: إِنَّهَا جِسْمٌ لَطِيفٌ شَفَّافٌ [؟]، الْحُرْمَةُ [؟]، بَلْ رُبَّمَا كَانَ كُفْرًا، وَالْجَوَابُ: أَنَّ هَذَا لَيْسَ مَقْصُودًا وَلا مُرَادًا.
And do not delve into the soul, since there has not come a text from the Lawgiver, but it is found. And when people differed concerning the soul also into two groups: a group that refrained from speaking about it because it is a secret from His secrets, Exalted is He, whose knowledge has not been given to humans, and this way was the chosen one; the versifier began decisively with it and said: (And do not delve) we, the community of the majority of verifiers, (into) the explanation of the reality (of the soul) with a genus and a specific difference distinguishing it; due to the impossibility of ascertaining them; because of the absence of transmitted revelation concerning them, and they are not received except from Him. And he pointed to the reason for the prohibition of delving into it in this way, that it is contrary to proper conduct with the Lawgiver since He did not explain it to His Prophet. By his saying: (since there has not come), i.e., our not delving into its explanation by way of recommendation, so delving into the explanation of its reality is disliked; due to the lack of authorized explanation in that; since it is among the unseen things that are not known except from the Lawgiver, and there has not come (a text) i.e., a proof (from the Lawgiver) – and He is Allah Most High – explaining it; because our Prophet did not convey that to us from Him, and everything that is like that, then refraining from delving into it is more appropriate. And for this reason al-Junayd said: The soul is something that Allah has exclusively preserved His knowledge of, and He has not informed any of His creation about it; so it is not permissible for His servants to research about it beyond that it exists; Allah Most High said: {And they ask you about the soul. Say: The soul is of the command of my Lord} [al-Isra: 85], i.e., from what Allah has exclusively preserved His knowledge of; as a demonstration of the incapacity of the person since he does not know the reality of his own self that is between his sides despite the certainty of its existence, so he refers knowledge back to Him, Exalted is He, with acknowledgment of incapacity to comprehend what Allah has not informed him of. And upon this way are Ibn Abbas and the Salaf, and upon it rests refraining from making a definite judgment about a specific location for it in the body. And the Prophet did not leave this world until Allah had informed him of all that He had left ambiguous for him; but He commanded him to conceal some and to make known the others. And the second group spoke about it and researched its reality. al-Nawawi said: The most correct thing said about it in this way is what Imam al-Haramayn said: That it is a subtle, transparent body [؟], sanctity [؟], rather it might be disbelief, and the answer: That this is neither intended nor desired.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling on discussing the essence of the soul (al-ruh). The author begins by quoting the poetic line: 'Do not delve into the soul, since a text from the Lawgiver has not come, but it is found.' He then notes that people differed into two groups: one refrained from speaking about the soul's reality, considering it a divine secret not given to humans, and the author states that this is the chosen way. The versifier (the poet) strongly endorsed this position. The author explains that the prohibition from delving into it is due to lack of proper conduct with the Lawgiver, who did not disclose it to the Prophet. The phrase 'since there has not come' indicates that our abstention from explanation is recommended, so delving is disliked (makruh) because no authorized explanation exists. The soul is among the unseen matters known only from the Lawgiver. Since the Prophet did not convey its essence, it is better to refrain. Al-Junayd is quoted: the soul is something Allah kept exclusively to Himself, so research is limited to affirming its existence. The Qur'anic verse (17:85) is cited: 'They ask you about the soul. Say: The soul is of the command of my Lord,' meaning it is among Allah's exclusive knowledge, demonstrating human incapacity. Ibn Abbas and the Salaf followed this way, refraining from specifying a location for the soul in the body. The Prophet was informed of all such matters but commanded to conceal some. The second group discussed it and researched its reality. Al-Nawawi reports that Imam al-Haramayn said it is a subtle transparent body, but the author notes (possibly through a gloss) that such investigation may be prohibited or even disbelief, and the answer is that such speculation is not intended nor desired. Throughout, the author emphasizes the proper methodology: avoid speculation where revelation is silent.

Key Terms
الروح (al-ruh)
الخوض (al-khawd)
الشارع (al-shari')
المغيبات (al-mughayyabat)
الوقف (al-waqf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think it is permissible to research the soul's essence as a theological matter.
Correction: The passage shows it is disliked (makruh) because no revealed text exists about it; it is part of the unseen known only to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the ruling on investigating the reality of the soul (al-ruh)?
A) It is obligatory because the soul is a fundamental belief.
B) It is permissible as long as it is based on reason.
C) It is disliked (makruh) because no text has come from the Lawgiver explaining it.
D) It is forbidden (haram) absolutely because it leads to disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Delving into the soul's essence without revealed text is disliked, and the correct method is to affirm its existence without speculation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you find yourself most prone to speculating about things Allah has not revealed—your future, the unseen, the nature of the soul itself? What step will you take this week to surrender that speculation and trust His hidden wisdom?
?
The Prophet ﷺ was not told the soul's essence until after his mission, and even then he concealed some knowledge. If the Prophet himself accepted limitations in knowledge, how does that challenge your own intellectual pride? Will you confess to Allah a specific area where you've acted as if you know what only He knows?
?
You affirm that the soul is a secret of Allah. But does your heart truly rest in that, or do you secretly wish you could uncover it? What would change in your relationship with Allah if you fully embraced the beauty of His concealed secrets?

Unseen Matters
beginner
5 min
The Ruling on Discussing the Soul's Reality
Sanusi rules on discussing the soul and provides proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why discussing the soul's reality is disliked and that only its existence is known through revelation, not its true nature.

Core Idea
The soul's reality is exclusive to Allah; discussing it is disliked, not forbidden.

This lesson examines a key issue in Islamic theology: the soul's nature. Sanusi explains that while the soul's existence is known, its reality is not for us to investigate. He provides scriptural evidence and the stance of early scholars, showing the proper adab towards matters of the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the words of Sanusi and the Quranic verse settle before analyzing.
Notice how the author starts with the ruling.
2
Identify the ruling and its reason
Find the ruling on discussing the soul's reality. The passage states it is disliked (makruh) because there is no revealed text (tawqif) on it. Understand the difference between haram and makruh here.
The reason is lack of scriptural guidance, not a prohibition.
3
Examine the Qur'anic proof
Read the verse from Surah al-Isra (17:85) and the author's interpretation. Reflect on how the verse indicates that the soul's true nature is known only to Allah.
The verse shows human limitation.
4
Understand the scholarly stance
Note the quote from Junayd and the mention of Ibn Abbas and the Salaf. They all refrained from speculating about the soul's reality. Also consider the final point about the Prophet's knowledge.
This reflects the principle of tawqif.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإمساك عن الخوض في حقيقة الروح من قبل عن عدم خوضنا في بيانها على سبيل الندب، فالخوض في بيان حقيقتها مكروه؛ لعدم التوقيف في ذلك؛ إذ هي من المغيبات التي لا تعرف إلا الشارع، ولم يرد نص عن الشارع وهو الله تعالى ببيانها؛ لأن نبينا لم يبلغنا ذلك عنه، وكل ما هو كذلك فالأولى الكف الخوض فيه، ولذا قال الجنيد: الروح شيء استأثر الله بعلمه، ولم يطلع عليه أحدًا من خلقه، فلا يجوز لعباده البحث عنه بأكثر من أنه موجود؛ قال الله تعالى: ﴿ويسألونك عن الروح قل الروح من أمر ربي﴾ [الإسراء: 85]، أي: مما استأثر الله بعلمه؛ إظهارا لعجز المرء حيث لم يعلم حقيقة نفسه التي بين جنبيه مع القطع بوجوده، فيرد العلم إليه سبحانه مع الإقرار بالعجز عن إدراك ما لم يطلعه الله عليه، وعلى هذه الطريقة ابن عباس والسلف، ويجري عليها الوقف عن الجزم بمحل مخصوص له من البدن، ولم يخرج النبي الدنيا حتى أطلعه الله على جميع ما أبهمه عنه؛ لكنه أمر بكتم البعض والإعلام بالبعض الآخر.
Refraining from delving into the reality of the soul is a non-binding recommendation. Delving into the explanation of its reality is disliked due to the lack of a revealed text regarding that, for it is among the unseen matters which are only known by the Lawgiver. No text came from the Lawgiver - and He is Allah Almighty - explaining it, because our Prophet did not convey that to us from Him. Everything that is such, it is better to refrain from delving into it. Hence Junayd said: 'The soul is a thing that Allah has kept exclusively to His knowledge, and has not informed any of His creation of it.' So it is not permissible for His servants to research it beyond that it exists. Allah Most High said: 'And they ask you about the soul. Say: The soul is of the command of my Lord' (al-Isra 17:85), i.e., from what Allah has kept exclusively to His knowledge, to show man's inability, as he does not know the reality of his own soul that is between his sides while being certain of its existence, so he refers knowledge back to Him, Exalted, while acknowledging inability to comprehend what Allah has not informed him of. On this method were Ibn Abbas and the Salaf. Based on it is refraining from affirming a specific location in the body for it. The Prophet did not leave this world until Allah had informed him of all that He had kept ambiguous from him, but he was ordered to conceal some and inform of others.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and proof. The author begins by stating that refraining from delving into the reality of the soul is a recommended practice, and that actually investigating its reality is disliked (makruh). The reason is that there is no revealed text (tawqif) on this matter; the soul belongs to the unseen (ghaybiyat) which can only be known through revelation. No explicit scriptural text from Allah explains its reality, and the Prophet did not convey such knowledge. Therefore, the best course is to abstain from discussion. The author then quotes the famous statement of Junayd al-Baghdadi that the soul is something Allah has kept exclusively to His knowledge. Consequently, it is not permissible for servants to research it beyond acknowledging its existence. The proof is the Qur'anic verse 'Say: The soul is of the command of my Lord,' which is interpreted by the author as meaning that the soul's true nature is from what Allah alone knows. This shows human inability, since a person does not know the reality of his own soul despite being certain of its existence. The stance of Ibn Abbas and the Salaf is cited, and it is noted that one should not affirm a specific location for the soul in the body. Finally, it is clarified that the Prophet was informed of all such concealed matters before his death, but he was commanded to keep some hidden and reveal others.

Key Terms
الروح
الإمساك
الخوض
المغيبات
التوقيف
الجنيد
السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the soul is unknown, all discussion about it is forbidden (haram).
Correction: The author clarifies it is disliked (makruh), not haram, and some knowledge like its existence is permissible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say discussing the reality of the soul is disliked (makruh)?
A) Because it leads to disbelief
B) Due to the lack of a revealed text (tawqif) on its reality
C) Because the Prophet explicitly forbade it
D) Because the soul does not exist

Key Takeaway
It is recommended to refrain from discussing the soul's reality beyond affirming its existence, as its true nature is known only to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know your own soul exists between your sides, yet you cannot grasp its reality. Does this not humble your pride in your own knowledge? How often do you act as if you possess complete understanding of yourself, forgetting that even your own soul is a mystery known only to Allah? What step will you take today to admit your ignorance and rely entirely on His revelation?
?
The Prophet knew the soul's reality but was commanded to conceal it. If the one who was most beloved to Allah kept silent on this, how can you feel entitled to speculate about matters beyond your reach? In what area of your life do you need to stop overstepping and simply submit to 'I don't know'?
?
Junayd said the soul is a thing Allah has kept exclusively to His knowledge. Does this not make you realize that true knowledge is what He reveals, not what you deduce? Are there matters of the unseen that you have been confidently discussing without scriptural basis? Will you now resolve to refrain from such speech, as the Salaf did?

The Soul as a Subtle Body: The Second Group's View
Sanusi presents the second group's view that the soul is a subtle body, with its definition and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second group's definition of the soul as a subtle, transparent, living body that intermingles with the dense body, and their scriptural proof from descriptions of movement.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle, living body interpenetrating the dense body like water in wood.

Having seen that the first group declined to define the soul, the second group engaged in research. Al-Nawawi reports that the soundest view is that of Imam al-Haramayn: the soul is a subtle body. In this passage, Sanusi presents that definition along with a key scriptural argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise definition and the analogy.
2
Identify the components of the definition
Look for the terms: 'subtle body', 'transparent', 'living by itself', 'interpenetrating'. Understand what each adds.
The analogy of water in green wood clarifies 'interpenetration'.
3
Understand the proof
The second group used scriptural descriptions of the soul descending, ascending, and moving in the barzakh. Why does this support a physical subtle body?
Movement implies a physical entity, not a pure spirit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفِرقة الثانية تكلَّمت فيها؛ وبحثت عن حقيقتها، قال النووي: وأصح ما قيل فيها على هذه الطريقة، ما قاله إمام الحرمين: إنها جسم لطيف شفاف، حي لذاته، مستبك بالأجسام الكثيفة اشتباك الماء بالعود الأخضر واحتجوا لهذا بوصفها بالهبوط والعروج والتردد في البرزخ.
The second group spoke about it and researched its reality. Al-Nawawi said: The soundest of what has been said about it in this way is what Imam al-Haramayn said: It is a subtle, transparent body, alive by itself, interpenetrating dense bodies like the interpenetration of water in green wood. They argued for this by describing it as descending, ascending, and moving to and fro in the barzakh.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage presents the view of the second group who did discuss the soul's reality. The author notes that even among them, the soundest opinion is that of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), as reported by al-Nawawi. Al-Haramayn defined the soul as a subtle body (jism latif), transparent, living by itself (hayy li-dhatihi), meaning its life is not derived from another soul to avoid infinite regress. It is interwoven with the dense body (mustabik bi al-ajsam al-kathifa) like the interpenetration of water in green wood, indicating a permeation of contact and indwelling, not mere adjacency. The proof for this view is the scriptural descriptions of the soul descending, ascending, and moving in the barzakh (intermediate realm), which implies it has a physical nature capable of movement. Thus, this group concluded it must be a physical but subtle entity.

Key Terms
جسم لطيف
حي لذاته
مستبك
اشتباك الماء بالعود الأخضر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul as a 'subtle body' means it is not a physical entity at all.
Correction: It is a physical body, but subtle (fine and transparent), not dense.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what proof did the second group use for the soul being a subtle body?
A) Its ability to think
B) Its being alive by itself
C) Its being described as descending, ascending, and moving in the barzakh
D) Its transparency

Key Takeaway
The second group, following Imam al-Haramayn, holds that the soul is a subtle, living body that interpenetrates the dense body, as supported by scriptural descriptions of its movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly experience your soul as a subtle reality interpenetrating your physical existence, or do you live as if you are merely a dense body? What would change if you consciously felt that your soul is a living entity that will one day ascend and face its Lord?
?
When you feel the weight of your worldly attachments, are you aware that your soul is meant to glide freely, descending only by divine permission? How can you loosen the grip of the body's desires to let your soul breathe?
?
The soul is described as 'alive by itself'—does your soul feel alive in you right now, or is it suffocated by negligence? What is one step you will take today to revive your soul's awareness of its subtle, eternal nature?

The Maliki View: Soul as Form
Sanusi relates a weak Maliki view on the soul and points out a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a weak opinion within the Maliki school that the soul is a form resembling the body, and the contradiction it raises about the number of souls.

Core Idea
The soul as a form questions whether each body has one soul or two.

Sanusi presents a view attributed to some Maliki scholars that the soul is a form resembling the body. This view, though considered weak, is included because of its connection to a deeper debate about the soul's nature and multiplicity. We will examine the definition and the problem it creates.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the technical terms like 'طريقة مرجوحة'.
2
Identify the weak method
Look for the phrase 'طريقة مرجوحة' and understand that this view is not definitive.
Sanusi explicitly labels it as weak.
3
Understand the definition of soul
The passage defines the soul as a 'form' (صورة) – a body with a shape like the physical body.
Note the description includes darkness, density, and subtlety.
4
Spot the contradiction
Read the last part where the author says this view may imply only one soul per body, which contradicts al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam's statement of two souls.
This shows internal disagreement among scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه الطريقة المرجوحة التي حكاها بقوله: (لكن وجدا لمالك) أي: لأهل مذهبه، ممن خاض في بيان حقيقتها هي يعني: روح كل جسد صورة أي: جسم ذو صورة كالجسد أي: كصورته في الشكل والهيئة، الظلمة والكثافة والرقة واللطافة، وتخصيص أهل مذهب مالك بالذكر لأنهم أتقى أرباب المذاهب للشبهات وأشدهم محافظة على النصوص الشرعية، وربما يفهم من قوله: صورة عدم تعدد الروح في كل جسد؛ فيكون مخالفا لما صرح به العز ابن عبد السلام من أن في كل جسد روحين.
And this is the weak method which he related by his saying: 'But there was found for Malik' i.e., for the people of his school, among those who delved into its reality. 'It' means: the soul of each body is a form, i.e., a body possessing a form like the body, i.e., like its form in shape and appearance, darkness and density, delicacy and subtlety. He specifies the people of Malik's school because they are the most God-fearing of the schools regarding doubts and the most protective of the religious texts. It may be understood from his saying 'form' that there is no multiplicity of the soul in each body; thus it contradicts what al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam stated, that in each body there are two souls.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage discusses a weaker opinion attributed to the Maliki school. The phrase 'but there was found for Malik' refers to those among his followers who investigated the soul's reality. They defined the soul as a form (sura) – a body having a shape resembling the physical body in shape and appearance, in terms of darkness/density and subtlety. The author notes that Malik's school is singled out because they are the most cautious regarding doubts and most protective of religious texts. However, a problem arises: if the soul is a form identical to the body, it implies only one soul per body, which contradicts the explicit statement of al-Izz ibn Abd al-Salam that there are two souls in each body. This indicates a point of contention within the tradition.

Key Terms
طريقة مرجوحة
صورة
كالجسد
تعدد الروح
العز ابن عبد السلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The view that the soul is a form like the body is the established Maliki position.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly calls this a weak method (طريقة مرجوحة) and notes it is only found among some followers of Malik.

Checkpoint

D) It goes against the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
This passage presents a weaker Maliki opinion on the soul and highlights a contradiction about the number of souls.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that even respected scholars like the Malikis held a weak view on the soul, does it unsettle your trust in scholarly authority? How do you handle the discomfort of knowing that great minds disagreed on such a fundamental matter? Will you seek to understand the reasons behind each view or simply accept the stronger one based on trust?
?
This passage presents a contradiction between two positions on the soul. Have you ever held a belief about your own soul—its nature, its origin—without ever questioning where that belief came from? What would it cost you to honestly examine whether that belief is based on strong evidence or just inherited opinion?
?
The author calls the Maliki view 'weak' (marjuha). Think of a time you clung to an opinion because it came from someone you respected, even though deep down you sensed it was weak. What would it take for you to let go of that opinion and embrace a stronger one, even if it means admitting error?

The Maliki Definition of the Soul as a Subtle Body
The commentator presents the Maliki school's definition of the soul as a picture resembling the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maliki school's definition of the soul as a subtle body and the evidence used to support it.

Core Idea
The soul, according to Malikis, is a subtle body interpenetrating the physical body like water in green wood.

This lesson examines a passage from al-Sanusi's commentary that presents the Maliki position on the nature of the soul. The Maliki school defines the soul as a 'picture' (surah) — a subtle body that permeates the dense body. The commentator notes that this view is based on scriptural descriptions of the soul's ascent and descent, though he considers it a weaker position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read its literal translation to grasp the core meaning.
Notice the analogy of water and green wood.
2
Understand the analogy of water and green wood
Reflect on how water permeates green wood without becoming wood. This illustrates the soul's interpenetration of the body.
The soul is described as 'alive by itself' and 'intertwined' with the body.
3
Reflect on the Maliki school's approach
Consider why the Maliki school is singled out as most pious and protective of texts. How does this relate to their definition?
The commentator calls this method 'weak' but attributes it to Malik's followers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات حي لِذَاته (۱) ، مُسْتَبِك بالأجسام الكثيفة اشتباك الماء بالعُودِ الأخضر واحتجوا لهذا بوصفها بالهبوط والعُروج والتردد في البرزخ، وهذه الطريقة المرجوحة التي حَكَاهَا بقوله: (لَكِنْ وُجِدَا لِمَالِكِ) أي: لأهل مذهبه (۳)، ممن خاض في بيان حقيقتها (هي)(٤) يعني: روحُ كلَّ جسدٍ (صُورَةٌ) ، أي : جسم ذو صورة (كَالجَسَدِ)، أي: كصورته في الشكل والهيئة ، الظُّلمة والكثافة والرّقة واللطافة ، وتخصيص أهل مذهب مالك بالذكر .. لأنهم أتقى أرباب المذاهب للشُّبهات وأشدُّهم محافظة (ه) على النصوص الشَّرعيَّة
Section on Sam'iyyat (matters known through revelation). Alive by itself (1), intertwined with dense bodies like the intertwining of water with green wood. They argued for this by describing it with descent, ascent, and movement in the Barzakh. This is the weak method that he narrated with his saying: 'But it is found for Mālik (3),' i.e., for the people of his school (3), among those who delved into explaining its reality. 'It (4)' means: the soul of every body is a picture (ṣūrah), i.e., a body possessing a form, like the body (ka-al-jasad), i.e., like its form in shape and appearance, darkness and density, subtlety and fineness. The specification of the people of Mālik's school in mentioning is because they are the most pious of the people of the schools regarding doubts and the most protective (5) of the revealed texts.

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of the soul according to the Maliki school of thought. The commentator begins by quoting the poetic line that describes the soul as 'alive by itself' and 'intertwined with dense bodies like water intertwined with green wood' — an analogy for its permeation. The proponents of this view adduce scriptural descriptions of the soul's descent, ascent, and movement in the Barzakh. This method, the commentator notes, is a weak one (al-ṭarīqah al-marjūḥah) that the poet referred to with his words 'but it is found for Malik.' 'Malik' here refers to his school, who have delved into explaining its reality. The soul of every body is a 'picture' (ṣūrah), meaning a body with a form, similar to the physical body in shape and appearance, though different in darkness/density vs. subtlety. The commentator then explains that the Maliki school was singled out because they are the most God-fearing among the schools regarding doubts and most protective of the revealed texts.

Ibn Abd al-Salam's Two Souls and Refraining from Inquiry
The commentator contrasts Ibn Abd al-Salam's two‑soul theory and advocates refraining from inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Abd al-Salam's view that every body contains two distinct souls and why the commentator insists we must refrain from investigating the soul's essence further.

Core Idea
The soul's reality is beyond human grasp; we must refrain from delving into it.

In this passage, the commentator flags a potential contradiction with a previous statement and then introduces Ibn Abd al-Salam's doctrine of two souls. He describes each soul's function and emphasizes that their exact location is known only to Allah. The overall lesson is a call to theological restraint: we should not overstep our limits by speculating about the soul's true nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text once, then its literal translation. Let the words of the commentator and Ibn Abd al-Salam settle before analyzing.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الإمساك عن الخوض'.
2
Identify the two souls and their roles
Locate where Ibn Abd al-Salam describes each soul. Note how the soul of wakefulness relates to sleep and dreams, and the soul of life relates to death and resurrection.
The two souls work side by side, like two fetuses in one womb.
3
Reflect on the command to refrain
Consider why the commentator advocates refraining from further inquiry. What does this tell you about the limits of rational investigation in matters of the soul?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يُفهم من قوله : «صورة» عدم تعدُّد الرُّوح في كُلِّ جسد ؛ فيكون مخالفا لما صرّح به العِزُّ ابن عبد السلام (۱) مِن أنّ في كُلِّ جَسدٍ رُوحَيْن : الإمساك عن الخوض في حقيقة الروح إحداهما : روح اليقظة التي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بأنَّها إذا كانت في الجسد كان الإنسان مستيقظا ، فإذا خرجت منه نام الإنسان (۱) ، ورأتُ تلك الرُّوحُ المنامات . والأخرى: روح الحياة التي أجرى الله تعالى العادة بأنها إذا كانت في الجسد كان حيًّا ، فإذا فارقته ماتَ ، فإذا رجعت إليه حَيي. وهاتان الرُّوحان في باطن الإنسان لا يعرفُ مقرَّهُما إِلَّا مَن أطلعه الله على ذلك، فهما كجنينين في بطن امرأة واحدة، والله أعلم(٢).
And perhaps it may be understood from his saying 'picture' that there is no multiplicity of the soul in every body; so it would contradict what ʿIzz al-Dīn ibn ʿAbd al-Salām (1) explicitly stated: that in every body there are two souls: refraining from delving into the reality of the soul. One of them is the soul of wakefulness (rūḥ al-yaqẓah), regarding which God Almighty has established the custom that when it is in the body, the person is awake, and when it leaves him, the person sleeps (1), and that soul sees the dreams. The other is the soul of life (rūḥ al-ḥayāh), regarding which God Almighty has established the custom that when it is in the body, the person is alive, and when it departs from him, he dies, and if it returns to him, he lives. And these two souls are inside the human; no one knows their location except whom God has informed of that. They are like two fetuses in the womb of one woman, and God knows best (2).
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage presents the objection to the previous definition and the correct stance. The commentator notes that the word 'picture' might imply that there is no multiplicity of souls in a single body, which contradicts what ʿIzz al-Dīn ibn ʿAbd al-Salām explicitly stated: that in every body there are two souls. The commentator then states the rule: one must refrain from delving into the reality of the soul (al-im‘sāk ‘an al-khawḍ fī ḥaqīqat al-rūḥ). After this, he describes the two souls according to Ibn ʿAbd al-Salām. The first is the soul of wakefulness (rūḥ al-yaqẓah): God's custom is that when it is in the body, the person is awake, and when it leaves, the person sleeps and that soul sees dreams. The second is the soul of life (rūḥ al-ḥayāh): when it is in the body, the person is alive; when it departs, death occurs; if it returns, life returns. These two souls are within the human, and their precise location is known only to God. The commentator likens them to two fetuses in the womb of one woman. The passage ends with 'and God knows best.'

Key Terms
رُوح اليقظة
رُوح الحياة
الإمساك عن الخوض
ابن عبد السلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is a simple, indivisible entity that cannot be multiple in one body.
Correction: Ibn Abd al-Salam teaches that two distinct souls can coexist in a single body, each with different functions (wakefulness and life).

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Ibn Abd al-Salam's two-soul theory challenges a simplistic view of the soul, and the safest theological approach is to refrain from speculating about its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Were Souls Created Before Bodies? The Classical Debate
The commentator discusses two opinions on the temporal priority of souls over bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main scholarly opinions on whether souls were created before bodies or after, and the scriptural evidence cited for each view.

Core Idea
Was the soul created before the body? Scholars disagree.

This lesson examines a classical theological debate about the order of creation of souls and bodies. The commentator presents two well-known opinions, each supported by hadith and Quranic verses. Clarifying this issue helps understand Islamic beliefs about the pre-existence of souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the debate.
2
Identify the two opinions
Notice that the passage clearly states there are two opinions on the temporal priority of souls over bodies. List them with their proponents.
3
Examine the evidence for souls first
Study the hadith and Quranic references cited for the view that souls were created before bodies. Note the weakness of one hadith.
4
Examine the evidence for bodies first
Look at the verse from Surah al-Insan and the report about Adam's body waiting 40 years for the soul.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this debate matters for theology and how it relates to the nature of the soul.

Source Text

Benefit: There is disagreement regarding the precedence of the creation of souls over bodies and its delay, on two well-known opinions: As for the first: that souls are created before bodies, and it is the famous opinion. Muhammad ibn Ṣanr held it, and Ibn Ḥazm even claimed consensus on it. They adduced as evidence what Ibn Mandah extracted from the hadith of ʿAmr ibn ʿAbasah raised: that God Almighty created the souls of servants two thousand years before bodies; those which recognized each other became united, and those which did not recognize each other differed. Its chain is very weak. And also the hadiths about the extraction of the progeny of Adam from his back, including the hadith: 'When God created Adam, He wiped his back, and there fell from it every soul that He is to create until the Day of Resurrection, like atoms.' Al-Hakim extracted it from the hadith of Abu Hurayrah, and al-Hakim from Ubayy ibn Kaʿb regarding His saying: 'And when your Lord took from the Children of Adam' [al-Aʿraf: 172]. He said: 'He gathered them that day, all that is to happen until the Day of Resurrection, and made them souls and forms, and spoke to them, and they spoke, and He took from them the covenant and the pledge...' the hadith. And the second: that bodies are created before souls. They argued by His saying: 'Has there come upon man a period of time when he was not a thing mentioned?' [al-Insan: 1]. It is related that he remained forty years before the soul was breathed into him.
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This footnote discusses the scholarly disagreement on the temporal priority of the creation of souls relative to bodies. The two well-known opinions are presented. The first opinion holds that souls were created before bodies. This is the more famous view, attributed to Muḥammad ibn Ṣanr [?], and Ibn Ḥazm even claimed consensus on it. Evidence includes a ḥadīth (though with a weak chain) that God created the souls of servants two thousand years before bodies, causing love and aversion based on recognition. Another evidence is the ḥadīth about the extraction of progeny from Adam's back. The second opinion holds that bodies were created before souls. Evidence is the Qur'anic verse 'Has there come upon man a period of time when he was not a thing mentioned?' (76:1), and a report that Adam's body remained forty years before the soul was breathed into it.

Correction: The debate is only about the chronological order, not about ontological superiority.

Learning Objective

The Soul's Pre-Creation vs. Its Blowing into the Body
Hadith citation with a reconciliatory response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the creation of the soul (khalq al-ruh) and the blowing of the soul (nafkh al-ruh), and how this reconciles the hadith with the belief that souls pre-exist their embodiment.

Core Idea
The soul is created long before it is blown into the body at 120 days.

This lesson addresses a potential conflict between the famous hadith of Ibn Mas'ud and the theological position that souls exist prior to their entry into the body. The hadith describes the angel breathing the soul into the fetus after 120 days, which might suggest the soul is only then created. The author resolves this by carefully distinguishing between two distinct events: the creation of the soul and its blowing into the body. Let's examine the hadith and the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic hadith and its translation. Notice the stages: drop, clot, lump, then the angel is sent to blow the soul. Understand the apparent timeline.
2
Identify the objection
Consider how someone might conclude from this hadith that the soul is created at the 120-day mark. Write down why this could be a problem for the view that souls are pre-created.
3
Learn the answer: the distinction
Read the author's answer carefully. He distinguishes between 'the blowing of the soul' and 'the creation of the soul.' Understand that the blowing is the entry of the soul into the body, not its origination.
4
Reflect on the reconciliation
Consider how this distinction solves the apparent conflict. The hadith talks about when the soul enters the body, not when it is created. The soul existed before but was only sent to the body at that time.
This aligns with the broader theological position that souls are created before bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبحديث ابن مسعود : إن أحدكم يجمع خلقه في بطن أمه أربعين يوما نطفة ، ثم يكون علقة مثل ذلك ، ثم مضغة مثل ذلك ، ثم يُرسل إليه الملك فينفخُ فيه الروح» رواه البخاري ومسلم، وأجيب بالفرق بين نفخ الروح وخلقه، فالروح مخلوقة من زمان طويل، وأُرسلت بعد تصوير البدن مع الملك لإدخالها في البدن.
And by the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Indeed, the creation of each one of you is gathered in his mother's womb for forty days as a drop, then becomes a clot for that long, then a lump for that long, then the angel is sent to him and breathes into him the soul.' Reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim. And it was answered by distinguishing between the blowing of the soul and its creation: for the soul was created a long time ago, and was sent after the formation of the body together with the angel to enter it into the body.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential conflict: the hadith states that the soul is breathed into the fetus after 120 days, which might suggest the soul is created at that moment. The answer clarifies that there is a distinction between the blowing (nafkh) of the soul and its creation (khalq). The soul was actually created much earlier, and at the time of the blowing, it is merely sent along with the angel to enter the prepared body. Thus, the hadith does not imply that the soul is first created at that point; rather, it describes the event of its entry into the human body. This reconciles the hadith with the view that souls exist prior to their embodiment.

Key Terms
نفخ الروح
خلق الروح
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is created at the time of the blowing into the fetus (120 days).
Correction: The soul is created long before the blowing; the blowing is the moment it enters the body, not when it is originated.

Checkpoint

A) Between the blowing and the creation of the soul
B) Between the embryo and the fetus
C) Between the angel and the soul
D) Between the drop and the clot

Key Takeaway
The soul is created prior to its blowing into the body, so the hadith describes entry, not origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your soul was created long before you took physical form. If your soul is the real 'you', why do you spend almost all your energy serving your body—its cravings, its status, its comfort—while your soul waits neglected? ?
The hadith says the soul enters the body at 120 days. Have you ever thanked Allah for the angel who conveyed your soul into your body? Or have you lived as if you were only a biological accident? Write down one prayer you will say tonight acknowledging your soul's divine origin.
?
If your soul pre-exists your body, then your value is not in your health, appearance, or achievements. What have you been chasing that will mean nothing when your soul leaves this body? Commit to one action today that prioritizes your eternal soul over a temporary worldly gain.

The Seat of the Soul in Life and Its Abode After Death
The author lists opinions on the soul's location in the body and its final resting places, then states the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various opinions about the soul's location in life and after death, and the correct view that the soul is a subtle body pervading the entire body.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle body that permeates the whole body, not confined to one organ.

The text begins by warning against excessive speculation about the soul's reality. It then presents a survey of opinions: where the soul resides during life and after death. Finally, it endorses the view of Imam al-Haramayn that the soul is a subtle body intertwined with the whole body. This lesson navigates these views and clarifies the correct position.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening warning
Notice the caution: 'Restraining from delving into the reality of the soul.' This sets the tone that the soul's essence is beyond full human grasp.
The author acknowledges limits of knowledge.
2
Identify opinions during life
Find the three views: soul in abdomen, near heart, or with the heart. These reflect different scholarly interpretations.
3
Identify opinions after death
Learn about souls of the blessed at grave courtyards or in the barzakh with Adam, and souls of disbelievers at the well of Barhut.
The barzakh is an intermediate realm.
4
Understand the correct view
Focus on the description: the soul is a subtle body (jism latif) that permeates the entire body like water in green wood.
This view synthesizes the earlier opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإمساك عن الخوض في حقيقة الروح الحياة البطن، وقيل: بقرب القلب، وقيل به. وأما بعد الموتِ فأرواحُ السُّعداء بأفنية القبور، وقيل: في البرزخ عند آدم، وهي متفاوتة فيه أعظم تفاوت، وأرواحُ الكُفَّار بيئر برهوت، بحضرموت. الحقيقة ، والصواب ما قاله إمام الحرمين كما قاله والد الشارح، من أنها جسم لطيف مشتبك أي : متصل بالجسم - اشتباك - أي: اتصال ـ الماء بالعود الأخضر ... الخ، فهي سارية في جميع البدن.
Restraining from delving into the reality of the soul. During life: [some say] the abdomen, and it was said: near the heart, and it was said: with it. And after death: the souls of the blessed are at the courtyards of graves, and it was said: in the barrier (barzakh) with Adam, and they are vastly varying in it, and the souls of the disbelievers are at the well of Barhut in Hadramawt. The truth, and the correct view is what Imam al-Haramayn said, as the sharh's father said: that it is a subtle body intertwined, i.e., connected to the body - intertwinement meaning connection like water with green wood ... etc., so it pervades the entire body.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage presents a summary of views on the soul's location. The heading 'Restraining from delving into the reality of the soul' signals that one should not go too deep, but the author proceeds to outline some opinions. First, during life: some say the soul resides in the abdomen, others near the heart, others in the heart itself. After death: the souls of the righteous (the 'blessed') are at the courtyards of graves, and another opinion says they are in the barzakh with Adam, and within that realm they are vastly different in rank. The souls of disbelievers go to the well of Barhut in Hadramawt. Then the author states the truth according to Imam al-Haramayn (and endorsed by the commentator's father): the soul is a subtle body (jism latif) that is intertwined (mushtabik) with the body like water with green wood, meaning it permeates the entire body. This view synthesizes the various opinions: the soul is not confined to one spot but pervades all parts.

Key Terms
الروح
البرزخ
أفنية القبور
بئر برهوت
جسم لطيف
مشتبك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is confined to one specific organ like the heart or brain.
Correction: The correct view is that the soul is a subtle body that pervades the entire physical body.

Checkpoint

A) It is confined to the heart
B) It is a subtle body that pervades the entire body
C) It is located only in the abdomen
D) It is a pure spirit without connection to the body

Key Takeaway
The soul is a subtle body that permeates the entire physical body, and after death, souls dwell in specific places until the Day of Judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The soul pervades your entire body right now. Are you aware of its presence? Do you live as if your soul is a subtle, divine trust, or do you ignore it? What would change if you truly felt your soul in every part of you?
?
After death, your soul will go to a specific place based on your faith and deeds. Are you confident where you will be? What have you done today that affects your soul's final abode?
?
The author restrains from delving into the soul's reality, yet we must know enough to act. Are you content with not knowing everything, or do you demand certainty? How does this humility shape your journey?

The Nature of the Soul and Its Afterlife Abode
Definition of the soul and its post-mortal states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological position on the nature of the soul as a subtle body and its state in the afterlife, distinguishing between the souls of the righteous and the disbelievers.

Core Idea
The soul is a subtle body that permeates the physical; its afterlife abode differs for believers and disbelievers.

This lesson from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid discusses one of the samiyyat (matters of the unseen): the reality of the soul and its post-mortal state. The author navigates between caution against speculation and the correct doctrinal view, offering a tangible analogy for the soul-body relationship.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial caution
The passage begins by refraining from delving into the soul's reality, acknowledging the limits of human intellect and the recommended stance of silence on its essence.
This reflects the pious attitude of avoiding unnecessary speculation.
2
Explore opinions on the soul during life
Various opinions are mentioned about the soul's location in the living body: some say it is life in the belly, others near the heart, etc. (the text includes a corrupted marker '8X').
These views are not the final word; they show the range of early scholarly discussion.
3
Learn about afterlife abodes
After death, the souls of the fortunate (righteous) reside in the courtyards of graves or in the Barzakh with Adam, varying greatly in rank. The souls of disbelievers are confined to the well of Barhut in Hadramawt.
The Barzakh is the intermediate realm between this world and the Hereafter.
4
Grasp the correct view: subtle body
The commentator affirms Imam al-Haramayn's view: the soul is a subtle body (jism latif) interwoven with the physical body like water in green wood, permeating it entirely.
This reconciles the soul's immateriality with its connection to the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refraining from delving into the reality of the soul: it is life in the belly (1), and it is said: near the heart, and it is said 8X. As for after death, the souls of the fortunate (2) are in the courtyards of the graves (3), and it is said: in the Barzakh (4) with Adam, and they vary in it greatly (5). Al-Shanwani.

This passage defines the soul and its state after death. It begins by stating the recommended stance of refraining from speculation about its essence, yet it presents various opinions on its nature during life: some say it is life in the belly, others near the heart, etc. (with a corrupted marker '8X'). Then it describes the post-mortal condition: righteous souls reside in the courtyards of graves or in the Barzakh with Adam, varying in rank; disbelievers' souls are in the well of Barhut in Hadramawt. ?
The correct view is that the soul is a subtle body interwoven with the physical. This means your body and soul are inseparably linked. How does this affect your view of your own body—do you treat it as a mere shell or as the vehicle of your soul? What can you do to honor this connection in your daily actions?

The Obligation of Belief in the Questioning in the Grave
The author asserts the obligation of belief in the grave questioning and explains its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in the questioning of the grave is an obligatory article of faith based on revelation, and that it applies to the entire Ummah.

Core Idea
The questioning in the grave is a reality we must believe in, established by revelation, not reason.

This lesson addresses one of the sam'iyyat — matters known only through revelation. The author emphatically states that belief in the questioning of the grave, its bliss and punishment, is obligatory, just like belief in the Resurrection. Before we read the passage, it is important to realize that this belief is not derived from rational argument but is a matter of faith based on transmitted texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud or silently
Read the matn line and the explanation that follows. Notice the emphatic language: 'wajib' (obligatory) and 'sam'an' (by revelation).
2
Identify the core assertion
The passage states that our questioning in the grave is obligatory like the resurrection. This is not a matter of opinion but a required belief.
3
Learn who is questioned
The pronoun 'our' refers to the entire Ummah of da'wah, including believers, hypocrites, and disbelievers. The majority view includes all, even jinn.
Ibn Abd al-Barr disagreed, but the stronger position is the majority.
4
Reflect on the evidence
The obligation is based on hadiths that mention the testing in graves. These texts establish the reality of the questioning.
The passage cites several hadiths from Muslim and Ahmad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤال القبر ونعيمه وعذابه
سُؤَالُنا ثُمَّ عَذَابُ القَبْرِ نَعِيمُهُ واجِبْ كَبَعْثِ الحَشْرِ
ثُمَّ أشار إلى حكم واجب الاعتقادِ ؛ فقال: (سُؤَالُنَا)، أي: سؤالُ مُنْكَرٍ ونَكِيرٍ إيَّانا مَعَاشِرَ أُمَّة الدَّعوة المؤمنين والمنافقين والكافرين (۲) بعد إقعادِنا بعد تمام الدفن، وعند انصراف (۳) النَّاس (٤) ، واجب سمعا، بأن يعيد الله مع محلها الحقيقي، والله تعالى أعلم. (۱) قوله: (منكر ونكير منكر : بفتح الكاف اسم مفعول أنكر ، والقياس جواز الكسر لإنكاره على العاصي، ونكير بوزن مليك ، فهو فعيل بمعنى مفعول ، والقياس كونه فاعل أيضا من نكرت الرجل إذا لم تعرفه. وإنما سُميا بهذا الاسم لأن الميت لم يكن يعرفهما ولا رأى صورة مثل صورتهما ، قال المصنف : لأنهما لا يشبهان خلق الآدمي ولا خلق الملائكة، ولا خلق الطير، ولا خلق البهائم، ولا خلق الهوام، بل هما خلقٌ بديع، وليس في خلقهما للناظرين. الأمير والشنواني . (۲) الضمير في (سؤلنا) في كلام الناظم لأمة الدعوة ، فيدخل المؤمن والمنافق والكافر ولو جنا، وهذا ما عليه القرطبي والجمهور ، لحديث: إن هذه الأمة تُبتلى في قبورها» مسلم: (٢٨٦٧) وأحمد (٢١٧٠١). ولحديث: «أُوحي إليَّ أنكم تفتنون في قبوركم» أحمد (٢٦٠٠٨) وغيره. وحديث بي تفتنون وعني تسألون أحمد (٢٥۱۳۳) وغيره. وخالف ابن عبد البر في تمهيده: في أنَّ الكافرَ لا يُسأل ، وإنما يُسأل المؤمن والمنافق لانتسابه إلى الإسلام في الظاهر ، وخالف ابن القيم فقال: كل نبي مع أمته كذلك. وقال في التنبيه الثالث: أنَّ السؤال في القبر عن العقائد فقط . انظر : «هداية المريد» ج ۲ ص ٩٩٢ وما بعدها.
Question of the Grave, Its Bliss and Its Punishment. Our questioning, then the punishment of the grave and its bliss, is obligatory like the resurrection of the gathering. Then he pointed to a ruling of obligatory belief and said: 'Our questioning' i.e., the questioning by Munkar and Nakir of us, the community of the da'wah, believers, hypocrites, and disbelievers, after we are seated after completion of burial, and when the people depart, is obligatory by revelation (sam'an), that Allah returns... with its true locus, and Allah knows best. (1) His statement: (Munkar and Nakir): Munkar is with fatha on the kaf, the passive participle of ankara, and analogy permits kasra because of his disapproval of the sinner. Nakir is on the pattern of malik, it is fa'il in the sense of maf'ul, and analogy also makes it active from nakartu al-rajula when you do not know him. They were named thus because the deceased does not know them and has not seen a form like their forms. The author said: because they do not resemble the creation of man, nor angels, nor birds, nor beasts, nor creeping things, but they are a wonderful creation, and there is nothing like them for the onlookers. (2) The pronoun in (su'aluna) in the poet's words refers to the Ummah of the Da'wah, so it includes the believer, the hypocrite, and the disbeliever, even the jinn. This is the position of al-Qurtubi and the majority, based on the hadith: 'This Ummah will be tested in its graves' (Muslim, Ahmad), and the hadith: 'It was revealed to me that you will be tried in your graves' (Ahmad), and the hadith: 'You will be tried and about me you will be asked' (Ahmad). And Ibn Abd al-Barr disagreed in his Tamhid, saying that the disbeliever is not questioned, only the believer and the hypocrite are questioned because of their outward affiliation to Islam. And Ibn al-Qayyim disagreed, saying that every prophet's nation is likewise. And in the third notice: the questioning in the grave is only about beliefs. See: Hidaya al-Murid, vol. 2, p. 992 and beyond.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage deals with the belief in the questioning in the grave (su'al al-qabr), which is part of the sam'iyyat (matters known through revelation). The author states that belief in the questioning in the grave, its bliss, and its punishment is obligatory, just like the belief in the resurrection (ba'th al-hashr). The explanation clarifies that the questioning is by Munkar and Nakir, two angels who question the deceased after burial. The obligation is based on revelation (sam'an), not reason. The term 'Munkar and Nakir' is explained: their names derive from the fact that the deceased does not recognize them. They have a unique form unlike any creation. The passage also addresses the scope: the questioning applies to the entire Ummah of Muhammad (s) – believers, hypocrites, and disbelievers – as held by the majority. It cites hadiths to support this. There is a minority view by Ibn Abd al-Barr that disbelievers are not questioned, but the majority opinion is that they are. Ibn al-Qayyim adds that every nation is questioned by its own prophet. The passage also notes that the questioning in the grave is about beliefs (aqa'id) only.

Key Terms
سؤال القبر
منكر
نكير
واجب سمعا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think only Muslims (believers and hypocrites) are questioned in the grave.
Correction: The majority, including al-Qurtubi, hold that the questioning applies to all of the Ummah of Muhammad (s), including disbelievers and even jinn, based on explicit hadith.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, who is subject to the questioning in the grave?
A) Only believers.
B) Only believers and hypocrites.
C) All of the Ummah including disbelievers.
D) Only the prophets.

Key Takeaway
Belief in the questioning in the grave by Munkar and Nakir is a required article of faith for all Muslims, established by revelation and supported by authentic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will be seated in your grave and questioned by Munkar and Nakir. If you were asked right now, 'Who is your Lord?', would your answer come from true conviction or mere memorization? What will you do today to ensure your answer is alive in your heart?
?
The passage says the questioning is wajib like the resurrection. When was the last time you let the reality of the grave shape your decisions, large or small? What is one habit you would change if you truly believed you could be questioned in the grave within the next hour?
?
You will be alone in the grave with only your deeds. The angels' appearance is terrifying and unfamiliar. Do you feel prepared to meet them? What specific action can you take before you sleep tonight to strengthen your faith in this trial?

Etymology and Meaning of the Names Munkar and Nakir
Footnote explains the linguistic derivation and reason for naming the two angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the names Munkar and Nakir and why these angels are given such names.

Core Idea
The angels' names reflect their unfamiliar appearance to the deceased.

In this lesson, we explore the etymology of the two angels of the grave, Munkar and Nakir. The commentator explains both the grammatical forms of their names and the theological reason behind them. This passage highlights how even the names of these angels convey their role and the experience of the deceased.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify linguistic points
Notice how 'Munkar' can be read with fatha or kasra, and 'Nakir' is on the pattern of 'fa'eel' meaning either active or passive participle.
The passage gives both possibilities.
3
Understand the reason for naming
Realize that the names are given because the deceased does not recognize these angels and their appearance is unlike any other creation.
The author lists several types of creatures to emphasize uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (منكر ونكير منكر : بفتح الكاف اسم مفعول أنكر ، والقياس جواز الكسر لإنكاره على العاصي، ونكير بوزن مليك ، فهو فعيل بمعنى مفعول ، والقياس كونه فاعل أيضا من نكرت الرجل إذا لم تعرفه. وإنما سُميا بهذا الاسم لأن الميت لم يكن يعرفهما ولا رأى صورة مثل صورتهما ، قال المصنف : لأنهما لا يشبهان خلق الآدمي ولا خلق الملائكة، ولا خلق الطير، ولا خلق البهائم، ولا خلق الهوام، بل هما خلقٌ بديع، وليس في خلقهما للناظرين.
His saying: 'Munkar and Nakir' – Munkar, with fatha on the kaf, is the passive participle of 'ankara; analogically the kasr is permissible because of his denial of the sinner. And Nakir is on the pattern of 'malik', it is fa'eel in the meaning of maf'ool, and analogically it can also be active participle from 'nakartu ar-rajula' if you did not know him. They are only named this because the deceased did not know them and never saw a form like their form. The author said: Because they do not resemble the creation of humans, nor the creation of angels, nor the creation of birds, nor the creation of beasts, nor the creation of insects, rather they are a wondrous creation, and there is no likeness to their creation for the beholders.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological explanation of the names of the two angels of the grave, Munkar and Nakir. The commentator explains that 'Munkar' is derived from 'ankara' (to deny), and it can be read with fatha or kasra on the kaf, both being possible as a passive participle (one who is denied) or an active participle (one who denies). 'Nakir' follows the pattern of 'fa'eel' and can mean 'one who denies' or 'one who is unknown'. The key point is that these names are given because the deceased does not recognize them and has never seen any form like theirs. The author (Ibn `Ashir) adds that their appearance is entirely unique: they do not resemble any creature known to humans—not humans, angels, birds, beasts, or insects. They are a 'badī' (innovative) creation, unlike anything seen. This unfamiliarity adds to the terror of the questioning, as the soul encounters beings beyond its experience.

Key Terms
منكر
نكير
اسم مفعول
خلق بديع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nakir is a superlative form.
Correction: Nakir is on the pattern fa'eel meaning either active or passive participle, not a superlative.

Checkpoint
Why are the two angels named Munkar and Nakir?
A) Because they question the deceased about good and evil deeds.
B) Because their appearance is unlike any creature and the deceased does not recognize them.
C) Because they are harsh and unpleasant.
D) Because they are responsible for punishment.

Key Takeaway
The names Munkar and Nakir highlight the unfamiliar and terrifying appearance of the angels who question the deceased in the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You will one day be questioned in your grave by beings unlike anything you have ever seen. Does that thought disturb you enough to change how you live today? What is one specific habit you will start or stop this week because of this reality?
?
The deceased had never seen such forms—the terror partly stems from the unfamiliar. Are you avoiding thinking about the unseen matters because they feel distant or uncomfortable? What will you do today to bring the remembrance of the grave into your daily life?

Who Is Questioned in the Grave?
Footnote discusses who is questioned: believers, hypocrites, and disbelievers of the ummah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view on who is subjected to the questioning in the grave, including the inclusion of hypocrites, disbelievers, and even jinn, along with key dissenting opinions.

Core Idea
The grave's questioning includes all who received the Prophet's call, not only believers.

This passage clarifies an important detail about the questioning in the grave. While many assume only believers are questioned, the majority view includes hypocrites, disbelievers, and even jinn who are accountable. Understanding this helps us appreciate the comprehensive nature of divine accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the terms used: المؤمن, المنافق, الكافر, and even جنا. This sets the stage for who is included.
2
Identify the groups included
The passage states that the questioning includes believers, hypocrites, disbelievers, and even jinn. This is the position of the majority (الجمهور) based on several hadiths.
The hadiths are general, referring to 'this ummah' and 'you'.
3
Explore dissenting views
The passage mentions two opposing views: Ibn 'Abd al-Barr limits questioning to those who outwardly affiliate with Islam (believers and hypocrites), while Ibn al-Qayyim extends it to every prophet's nation.
A third notice is that questioning is only about beliefs, not actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضمير في (سؤلنا) في كلام الناظم لأمة الدعوة ، فيدخل المؤمن والمنافق والكافر ولو جنا، وهذا ما عليه القرطبي والجمهور ، لحديث: إن هذه الأمة تُبتلى في قبورها» مسلم: (٢٨٦٧) وأحمد (٢١٧٠١). ولحديث: «أُوحي إليَّ أنكم تفتنون في قبوركم» أحمد (٢٦٠٠٨) وغيره. وحديث بي تفتنون وعني تسألون أحمد (٢٥۱۳۳) وغيره. وخالف ابن عبد البر في تمهيده: في أنَّ الكافرَ لا يُسأل ، وإنما يُسأل المؤمن والمنافق لانتسابه إلى الإسلام في الظاهر ، وخالف ابن القيم فقال: كل نبي مع أمته كذلك. وقال في التنبيه الثالث: أنَّ السؤال في القبر عن العقائد فقط .
The pronoun in 'our questioning' in the poet's speech is for the ummah of da'wah, so it includes the believer, the hypocrite, the disbeliever, even if a jinni. This is the view of al-Qurtubi and the majority, based on the hadith: 'This ummah is tried in its graves' (Muslim 2867, Ahmad 21701), and the hadith: 'It was revealed to me that you will be tested in your graves' (Ahmad 26008 and others), and the hadith: 'You will be tested and you will be asked about me' (Ahmad 25133 and others). Ibn 'Abd al-Barr in his Tamhid opposed: that the disbeliever is not questioned, only the believer and hypocrite are questioned because of their apparent affiliation to Islam. And Ibn al-Qayyim opposed and said: Every prophet with his nation is like that. And he said in the third notice: that the questioning in the grave is only about beliefs.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage addresses a key theological question: who exactly is subjected to the questioning in the grave? The commentator clarifies that the pronoun 'na' in 'su'aluna' (our questioning) in the poem refers to the 'ummah ad-da'wah' – the community that received the Prophet's call. This includes not just believers but also hypocrites and even disbelievers, and the text adds 'even a jinni' (a non-human who is accountable). This is the position of the majority of scholars, including al-Qurtubi, and is supported by several ahadith that use general terms like 'this ummah' and 'you' addressing the companions. However, there are two notable dissenting views mentioned: Ibn 'Abd al-Barr holds that only those who outwardly affiliate to Islam (believers and hypocrites) are questioned, not outright disbelievers. Ibn al-Qayyim, on the other hand, extends the questioning to all prophets and their nations, meaning every community that received a prophet will be questioned in its graves. Additionally, a third notice is cited: the questioning only concerns beliefs (aqaa'id), not actions, though this is not elaborated here.

Key Terms
أمة الدعوة
المؤمن
المنافق
الكافر
القرطبي
ابن عبد البر
ابن القيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only Muslims (believers) are questioned in the grave.
Correction: The majority view includes hypocrites, disbelievers, and even jinn who are accountable, because they all belong to the ummah of da'wah.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view in this passage, who is included in the questioning in the grave?
A) Only believers
B) Believers and hypocrites
C) Believers, hypocrites, and disbelievers of the ummah
D) Only the companions of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The grave's questioning extends to all members of the Prophet's ummah, including hypocrites and disbelievers, according to the majority of scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The questioning in the grave includes even the hypocrite. If your faith is only outward, what will your answer be when asked about your Prophet? Are you truly sincere, or just performing?
?
Ibn 'Abd al-Barr argued that only those who outwardly affiliate to Islam are questioned. Does your faith go beyond outward affiliation? How would you know if you are merely a hypocrite in your own heart?
?
The majority view holds that even disbelievers are questioned. Does this increase your urgency to share the truth, knowing that those who reject will also be held accountable? What are you doing now to ensure their hearing of the message?

The Grave's Questioning: Obligatory by Revelation
Commentator explains the ruling and mechanism of the grave's questioning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the questioning in the grave by the angels Munkar and Nakir is an obligatory belief established by revelation (sam'an), not by reason, and you will grasp the key details of how it occurs according to the majority of scholars.

Core Idea
The grave's questioning is a revealed obligation, not a rational one.

This lesson takes you through a key doctrine of the afterlife: the questioning in the grave. Sanusi's commentator explains the poet's reference to this belief, clarifying its ruling and the manner in which it takes place. The passage emphasizes that this is not a matter reason can deduce; it must be accepted from divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full picture of what the commentator is explaining about the grave's questioning.
Notice the technical terms like 'واجب سمعا' (wajib sam'an).
2
Focus on the ruling
The passage states that the questioning is 'wajib sam'an' (obligatory by revelation). Understand what this means and why it is an important distinction from rational obligations.
3
Learn the mechanism
The commentator explains how Allah returns the soul and completes the senses so the deceased can understand and answer. Also note the angels' treatment and the use of the person's own language.
This shows the wisdom and mercy of Allah in the questioning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ أشار إلى حكم واجب الاعتقادِ؛ فقال: (سُؤَالُنَا)، أي: سؤالُ مُنْكَرٍ ونَكِيرٍ إيَّانا مَعَاشِرَ أُمَّة الدَّعوة المؤمنين والمنافقين والكافرين بعد إقعادِنا بعد تمام الدفن، وعند انصراف النَّاس، واجب سمعا، بأن يعيد الله تعالى الرُّوحَ إلى الميت جميعه كما ذهب إليه الجمهور، وهو ظَاهِرُ الأحاديثِ، وتُكمَّلُ حَواسه، فَيَرُدُّ الله إليه ما يتوقف عليه فهم الخطاب، ويتأتى معه رَدُّ الجواب من الحواس والعقل والعلم، حتّى يسأله الملكان أو أحدهما، ويأخذ الله بأبصار الخَلائِق وأسماعهم، إلَّا مَن شاء الله عن حياة الميّت وما هو فيه عينًا وسماعاً، يَترفّقان بالمؤمن، وينتهران المُنافق والكافر، ويَسألان كُلّ أحدٍ بلسانه.
Then he pointed to the ruling of obligatory belief; so he said: 'Our questioning' – that is, the questioning of Munkar and Nakir of us, the community of the da'wah, the believers, hypocrites, and disbelievers, after our being seated after the completion of burial, and when the people depart, is obligatory by revelation (sam'an), in that Allah the Exalted returns the entire soul to the deceased as the majority holds, and that is apparent from the narrations. And He completes his senses, so Allah returns to him what is necessary for understanding the address, and with that the response occurs from the senses, intellect, and knowledge, until the two angels or one of them question him. And Allah takes away the sight and hearing of the creatures except whom He wills from the life of the deceased and what he is in, as a reality and hearing. They treat the believer gently, and they rebuke the hypocrite and disbeliever, and they question each one in his own tongue.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage explains the poem line 'Our questioning, then the punishment of the grave and its reward, is obligatory like the resurrection of the gathering.' The commentator begins by stating that the poet has now pointed to a matter of obligatory belief: the questioning in the grave by the two angels Munkar and Nakir. This questioning applies to the entire ummah of the da'wah – that is, the community of the Prophet Muhammad – including believers, hypocrites, and even disbelievers (as will be clarified later). The questioning occurs after the deceased is seated upright following burial and after the people have left, as described in hadith. The ruling is that it is wajib sam'an, meaning its obligation is established by revelation (the Qur'an and Sunnah), not by reason alone. The commentator then explains the means by which the questioning takes place: Allah returns the entire soul to the body (the view of the majority, not just a part of it), completing those senses needed to understand the questions and formulate answers (hearing and speech, not necessarily all senses). He also mentions that Allah prevents the living from seeing or hearing the deceased's state except those He wills. The two angels treat the believer gently and are harsh with the hypocrite and disbeliever. They question each person in his own language, as a mercy from Allah so that no one is disadvantaged.

Key Terms
سؤال القبر
واجب سمعا
منكر ونكير
إعادة الروح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the grave's questioning is only for Muslims.
Correction: The text includes hypocrites and disbelievers of the ummah, as they are part of the community of the da'wah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of the questioning in the grave?
A) Wajib 'aqli (rationally obligatory)
B) Wajib sam'an (obligatory by revelation)
C) Mandub (recommended)
D) Ja'iz (permissible)

Key Takeaway
The questioning in the grave is a revealed truth that occurs after burial, with Allah restoring the soul and senses to enable the deceased to respond.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to be questioned by Munkar and Nakir right now, in your own tongue, what would your answer be? Does that answer align with how you are living today, or are you postponing a commitment you already know you need to make?
?
The passage says the angels treat the believer gently. Is your life evidence that you are building a relationship with Allah that deserves gentleness, or are you gambling on His mercy while neglecting clear obligations?
?
You have just read that Allah hides the reality of the grave from the living. If you truly believed you will be seated and questioned within hours of your burial, what would you change about your next 24 hours?
