Two Opinions on Re-creating Accidents and the Preponderance of Re-creating Individuals
Al-Shanwani presents two opinions on re-creating accidents and states that re-creating individuals is given preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opinions on re-creating accidents in the afterlife and why the re-creation of individual substances is the preferred view in Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Accidents may or may not be recreated, but individual bodies will be restored.

This passage from al-Shanwani's commentary on the Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a subtle theological question: are the accidents (non-essential attributes) that belonged to a person in this life recreated in the afterlife? There are two opinions on this. The author also highlights that the re-creation of the individual themselves (the substance) is the preferred view, setting the stage for further discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's phrasing sink in before you interpret.
Notice the verse number 99 – this is a line from the matn (core text).
2
Identify the two issues
The passage treats two separate but related issues: re-creation of accidents (a'rad) and re-creation of individuals (a'yan). Spot where each is mentioned.
The author states there are two opinions on accidents, and that individuals are given preponderance.
3
Understand the context of the two opinions
The passage explains that those who affirm re-creation of individuals differed on whether their accidents are also re-created. The author then quotes the text: 'concerning the permissibility of re-creating the accident subsisting in bodies, following its locus, there are two opinions.'
The phrase 'following its locus' means accidents depend on the body they inhere in.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why is this distinction important? It affects our understanding of personal identity and the nature of the afterlife. The preponderance of re-creating individuals affirms bodily resurrection, while leaving accidents open to debate.
This connects to broader discussions in Islamic eschatology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إعادة الأعراض والأعيان
٩٩ . وَفِي إِعَادَةِ العَرَض قولانِ ورُبِّحَتْ إعادةُ الأَعْيَانِ
ولما اختلف القائلون بإعادة الأعيان في إعادة أعراضها التي كانت قائمةً بها في الدُّنيا، أشار إليه بقوله: (وَفِي) جواز (إِعَادَةِ العَرَضِ) القائمِ بالأجسام تبعًا لمحله (قَوْلَانَ)
The Re-creation of Accidents and Individuals. 99. And concerning the re-creation of the accident (i'adat al-'arad) there are two opinions, and the re-creation of individuals (i'adat al-a'yan) is given preponderance. And when those who affirm the re-creation of individuals differed about the re-creation of their accidents that were subsisting in them in this world, he indicated by his statement: 'And concerning the permissibility of re-creating the accident subsisting in bodies, following its locus, there are two opinions.'
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents the issue of whether accidents (a'rad, the non-essential attributes) are re-created along with the body in the afterlife. The author (al-Shanwani) states that there are two opinions on the permissibility of re-creating accidents. He also notes that the re-creation of individuals (that is, the same bodies) is given preponderance (rujjiha). Then he explains the context: those who affirm the re-creation of individuals differed on whether the accidents that characterized them in this life are also re-created. The author then quotes the text: 'And concerning the permissibility of re-creating the accident subsisting in bodies, following its locus, there are two opinions.' The phrase 'following its locus' means that the accident depends on the body in which it inheres. The passage thus identifies a point of disagreement among theologians regarding the eschatological state of accidents.

Who Is Exempt from Bodily Decay?
The commentator specifies the scope of the disagreement about earth not consuming bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the doctrine that the earth does not consume bodies is not universal but applies only to specific individuals and entities explicitly named in scripture, based on transmitted evidence.

Core Idea
Not all bodies are immune to decay—only those specifically named in divine texts.

This passage clarifies a theological assertion by specifying its scope. While it is agreed that prophets' bodies are preserved from decay, other categories—like martyrs, scholars, and even cosmic entities—are also exempt based on textual evidence. The commentator emphasizes that this knowledge comes only from revelation, not reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the overall structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins with restricting the general disagreement, then lists categories of exemption, and ends with the principle of tawqifiyyah.
The passage uses 'tawqifiyyah' to indicate that this knowledge is derived only from revelation.

Identify the categories exempt
List all the groups mentioned: prophets, martyrs, mu'adhdhins, carriers of the Quran, sinless individuals, acting scholars, the Spirit, the coccyx, Paradise and Hell and their inhabitants, the Throne, the Footstool, the Tablet, and the Pen.
Note that both human and cosmic entities are included.
3
Understand 'tawqifiyyah'
The passage concludes that this matter is tawqifiyyah, meaning it is based solely on transmitted textual evidence, not rational deduction. Reflect on why this principle is important for understanding the scope of the doctrine.
This distinguishes it from matters that can be deduced by reason.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why isn't this a general rule for all believers? The specification prevents overgeneralization and emphasizes that such knowledge comes only from scripture.
This helps avoid misconceptions about the afterlife.

إعادة الأعراض والأعيان الخِلَافُ خُصَّا) أي : قيَّد (۱) بعض العلماء إطلاقه (۲) (بِالأَنْبِيَا) فإِنَّ الأرض لا تأكلُ أجسامهم، ولا تُبلي أبدانَهم اتفاقا (وَمَنْ عَلَيْهِمْ) أي: وخُصَّ أيضا بالأشخاص الذين ( نُصَّا ) أي : نَصَّ الشَّارِعُ على عدم أكل الأرض أجسامهم ؛ كالشهداء، والمؤذنين ،احتسابا ، وحامل القرآن (۳) ، ومَن لم يعمل خطيئة والعلماء العاملين، والرُّوح ، وعَجب الذنب، والجنَّة والنار (٤) وأهلهما، والعَرْش والكرسي ، واللوح والقلم ، والمسألة توقيفية.

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note on the qualification of a disagreement. It begins by stating that some scholars restrict the general disagreement (about the earth not consuming bodies) specifically to prophets, because it is agreed that the earth does not consume their bodies. It lists these categories: martyrs, mu'adhdhins, carriers of the Quran, sinless individuals, acting scholars, the Spirit (possibly Angel Gabriel or the human spirit), the coccyx (which remains from the body), Paradise and Hell and their inhabitants, the Throne, the Footstool, the Tablet, and the Pen. The passage concludes that this matter is tawqifiyyah (based solely on transmitted textual evidence, not rational deduction). The passage follows the order: first the restriction to prophets, then the expansion to other textually specified groups, ending with the principle that it is tawqifiyyah.

Two Opinions on the Re-creation of Accidents
Commentator presents two opinions on re-creation of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on whether accidents are re-created on the Day of Resurrection, and the reasoning behind the majority view.

Core Idea
Accidents are re-created like substances because both are equally within God's power.

In this passage, the commentator presents a key theological debate among those who affirm the resurrection of bodies: are the accidents that inhered in those bodies also restored? The answer distinguishes two positions, one affirming and one denying, with the majority — including Imam al-Ash'ari — affirming based on the parity of divine power over accidents and substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the full literal translation. Familiarise yourself with the two opinions mentioned.
The passage begins by stating the issue and then elaborates the first opinion in detail.
2
Identify the two opinions
Notice that the first opinion (majority and al-Ash'ari) affirms re-creation of accidents in their specific individuals, while the second denies it entirely.
The first opinion includes a detailed rationale about God's power; the second is simply stated as impossible.
3
Understand the majority reasoning
Focus on the key argument: the relation of accidents to God's power is like that of substances, and since substances are re-created, so are accidents. This is the core rationale.
Also note that the majority makes no distinction between types of accidents or between voluntary and involuntary ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٩٩ . وَفِي إِعَادَةِ العَرَض قولانِ ورُبِّحَتْ إعادةُ الأَعْيَانِ ولما اختلف القائلون بإعادة الأعيان في إعادة أعراضها التي كانت قائمةً بها في الدُّنيا، أشار إليه بقوله: (وَفِي) جواز (ه) (إِعَادَةِ العَرَضِ) القائمِ بالأجسام تبعًا لمحله (٦) (قَوْلَانَ) أحدهما مذهب الأكثرين، وإليه مَيْلُ إمامنا الأشعري له ، أنَّها تُعاد بأشخاصها التي ؟ كانت في الدُّنيا قائمةٌ بالجسم حال الحياة، لا فرق في الأعراض التي يطول بقاء نوعها كالبياض بقاء نوعها كالبياض (٢)، وبين غيرها كالأصوات ، ولا بين ما هو مقدورٌ للعبد كالضَّرب وغيره (۳) كالعلم والجهل ؛ لأنَّ نِسبَة الأعراض إلى قدرته تعالى كنسبة الأعيان إليها ، وقد قام الدليل على إعادتها ، فكذا أعراضها ( ٤ ) . وثانيهما: امتناع إعادتها(ه)
99. And concerning the re-creation of the accident there are two opinions, and the re-creation of substances is preponderant. And when those who affirm the re-creation of substances differed regarding the re-creation of their accidents that were subsisting in them in this world, he alluded to that by his saying: (And concerning the permissibility of) (the re-creation of the accident) subsisting in bodies in accordance with its locus (6) (two opinions). One of them is the way of the majority, and toward it inclines our Imam al-Ash'ari – that they are re-created in their specific individuals [?] that were in this world subsisting in the body during life, with no distinction between accidents whose type persists for a long time, like whiteness (whiteness of its type), and others like sounds, nor between what is within the servant’s power like hitting and others (3) like knowledge and ignorance; because the relation of accidents to His power – exalted is He – is like the relation of substances to it, and the evidence has been established for their re-creation, so likewise their accidents (4). And the second: the impossibility of their re-creation (5).
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal dispute about the re-creation of accidents on the Day of Resurrection. It begins by stating that there are two opinions on the re-creation of accidents, noting that the re-creation of substances (bodies) is the accepted view. The commentator explains that the disagreement is among those who already affirm the re-creation of substances; they differ whether the accidents that originally inhered in those substances are also re-created. The first opinion, held by the majority and favored by Imam al-Ash'ari, affirms that accidents are re-created exactly as they were in the world, with no distinction between permanent types like whiteness and temporary ones like sounds, nor between voluntary acts like hitting and involuntary states like knowledge. The reason given is that accidents are equally within God’s power as are substances, and since there is proof that substances are re-created, the same applies to their accidents. The second opinion denies the possibility of re-creating accidents. The passage follows a logical structure: statement of the issue, two opinions, elaboration of the first with its rationale, and a brief mention of the second.

القولان
الأكثرين
الأشعري
تُعاد بأشخاصها
قدرة الله
الدليل
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this dispute is about whether accidents exist at all
Correction: The dispute is specifically about whether the same specific accidents that existed in this world are re-created after death, not about their existence per se.

Checkpoint
According to the majority opinion, why are accidents re-created along with substances?
A) Because accidents are independent of God's power
B) Because their relation to God's power is the same as that of substances
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly says so
D) Because the second opinion is weak

Key Takeaway
The majority view holds that accidents are re-created because they are as much within God's power as substances, and the evidence for re-creation of substances applies equally to their accidents.

?
When you sin, do you treat it as a fleeting accident that disappears forever, or do you acknowledge that God will bring it back, just as He will bring back your body, and live with that accountability now?
?
The text states that accidents are re-created because they are equally within God's power. Does your daily life reflect a belief that your smallest deeds are held in God's power and will be returned to you, or do you act as if only the big moments matter?

Two Opinions on Renewing Accidents
Lays out the majority view on renewing accidents and the opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on the renewal of accidents in the hereafter and the reasoning behind the majority Ash'ari view.

Core Idea
Accidents are renewed like substances because God's power relates equally to both.

Sanusi introduces a key topic of sam'iyyat: whether the accidents (qualities) that perish with death are brought back in the afterlife. He presents two positions—the majority view affirming renewal and a minority view denying it—and gives the proof for the majority. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Ash'ari doctrine on bodily resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opinions
Read the passage and locate the two opinions on renewing accidents. Notice that the first is attributed to the majority and to Imam al-Ash'ari, and the second is mentioned briefly.
The second opinion is simply stated as 'impossibility' without elaboration.
2
Understand the scope of the majority view
The majority view applies uniformly to all accidents, with no distinction between long-lasting types (like whiteness) and short ones (like sounds), nor between those within human power (striking) and those not (knowledge).
This universality is central to the proof.
3
Grasp the proof by analogy
The proof states that accidents relate to God's power in the same way as substances do. Since substances are renewed in the afterlife, their accidents must also be renewed.
This analogy is the key logical foundation of the majority position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات لله أحدهما مذهب الأكثرين ، وإليه مَيْلُ إمامنا الأشعري له ، أنَّها تُعاد بأشخاصها التي [؟] كانت في الدُّنيا قائمةٌ بالجسم حال الحياة، لا فرق في الأعراض التي يطول بقاء نوعها كالبياض، وبين غيرها كالأصوات ، ولا بين ما هو مقدورٌ للعبد كالضَّرب وغيره كالعلم والجهل ؛ لأنَّ نِسبَة الأعراض إلى قدرته تعالى كنسبة الأعيان إليها ، وقد قام الدليل على إعادتها ، فكذا أعراضها. وثانيهما: امتناع إعادتها.
The section of sam'iyyat (things known from revelation). One of them is the doctrine of the majority, and towards it inclines our Imam al-Ash'ari, may God have mercy on him, that they (accidents) are renewed with their individuals which were in the world subsisting in the body during life, with no distinction between accidents whose type persists long like whiteness, and others like sounds, and no distinction between what is within the servant's power like striking and others like knowledge and ignorance; because the relation of accidents to His power, exalted is He, is like the relation of substances to it, and the proof has been established for their renewal, so likewise their accidents. And the second of them: the impossibility of their renewal.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage presents two positions on the renewal of accidents (i'ādat al-a'rāḍ) in the hereafter. The first and majority view, endorsed by Imam al-Ash'ari, holds that accidents are brought back in their very individuals (bi-ashkhāṣihā) that existed in the world. This applies uniformly across all accidents: there is no distinction between those that persist in type like whiteness and those that do not like sounds, nor between those within human acquisition like striking and those not like knowledge and ignorance. The proof given is that accidents relate to God's power in the same way as substances do, and since substances are renewed (as established by evidence), their accidents must also be renewed. The second view, mentioned only briefly, states that the renewal of accidents is impossible (imtinā' i'ādatihā). The author then notes that this second view is held by some of our (Ash'ari) scholars as well.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The majority view only applies to certain types of accidents, like long-lasting ones or those not within human power.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that there is no distinction between any accidents; all are renewed because God's power is equal over them all.

Key Takeaway
The majority Ash'ari view holds that all accidents are renewed in the hereafter because God's power over accidents is like His power over substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you live with the certainty that every fleeting detail of your existence—every word, every color you see—will be brought back by His power? How would your daily speech change if you truly believed that every sound you make will be renewed before Him?
?
The majority view says no distinction is made between persistent accidents like whiteness and transient ones like sounds. That means nothing is too small or too fleeting for God to recreate. Is there a part of your past—perhaps a sin you thought was forgotten—that you are trying to hide from the reality of resurrection? Will you repent now, knowing that even the most transient wrong will be brought back?
?
If the renewal of all accidents is based on God's absolute power, then your very ability to strike or to know is dependent on Him. Does your heart acknowledge that even your most 'personal' actions and thoughts are not truly your own? How can you cultivate gratitude for the power that sustains every moment of your existence?

Defining Accident and the Renewal of Its Individuals
Defines 'arad and clarifies the preferred position on its renewal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'arad (accident) in kalam and why the preferred position is that the very individuals of accidents are renewed, not new accidents.

Core Idea
An accident is dependent existence; its renewal means the same individuals return, not new ones.

In kalam, accidents are contingent properties that inhere in substances. This lesson will clarify the precise definition of an accident according to the mutakallimun and then explain the preferred view regarding their renewal: that it is the very same individuals (persons or selves) that are recreated, avoiding the problem of accident subsisting in accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of accident
Focus on the two equivalent definitions: 'that which is spatially extended dependently' and 'that which does not subsist by itself.' Understand that accidents need a substance to exist.
Note the Arabic terms: العَرَض, التحيّز, القيام بذاته.
2
Understand the phrase about renewal
The commentary explains that the author indicates preponderance for the first saying: 'renewal of the individuals (a'yan) of accidents.' Identify what 'individuals' means here.
The individuals are the accidents themselves, not new accidents.
3
Reflect on the two interpretations
The passage mentions two meanings of 'a'yan': either the persons/selves of accidents, or the opposite of 'others.' Both avoid the contradiction of an accident subsisting by itself.
This preserves the definition of accident as not self-subsisting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعَرَضُ عند المتكلمين : ما يتحيّز تابعا في تحيزه لغيره، وهو كقولهم : ما لا يقوم بذاته ، بل بغيره . وأشار إلى ترجيح القولِ الأَوَّلِ بقوله: (وَرُجِّحَتْ إِعَادَةُ الأَعْيَانِ)، أي: ورجّح جماعةٌ إعادةَ أعيان الأعراض ، والمراد بها الأشخاص والأنفُس ، أو مُقابل الأغيار ، وكلاهما لا يلزم منه القيام بالذات المنافي للعرضيّة .
And the accident ('araḍ) according to the mutakallimun (theologians): that which is spatially extended dependently in its spatial extension on another, and it is like their saying: that which does not subsist by itself, but by another. And he (the author) pointed to the preponderance of the first saying by his statement: 'And the renewal of the individuals (a'yān) is deemed preponderant,' that is, a group deemed preponderant the renewal of the individuals (a'yān) of accidents, and what is meant by it is the persons (ashkhāṣ) and selves (anfus), or opposite of others (a'ghyār), and neither of them entails subsistence by the self, which is contrary to accidentality.
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage provides two elements: first, a definition of 'araḍ (accident) in the terminology of the mutakallimun. It is defined as something that is spatially extended (yataḥayyazu) but in a dependent manner—its spatial extension is dependent on another (i.e., a substance). An equivalent expression is 'that which does not subsist by itself but by another.' This distinguishes accidents from substances, which subsist by themselves. Second, the passage indicates that the author (the commentator) prefers the first opinion (the majority view) by quoting the line from the matn: 'وَرُجِّحَتْ إِعَادَةُ الأَعْيَانِ' (and the renewal of the individuals is deemed preponderant). This is explained to mean that some scholars gave preference to renewing the very individuals (a'yān) of accidents, meaning the persons (ashkhāṣ) or selves (anfus) of the accidents—i.e., the accidents themselves rather than different accidents. Alternatively, it means renewing the accidents themselves as opposed to 'others' (a'ghyār). Both interpretations avoid the problem of an accident subsisting in an accident (which would contradict the definition of 'araḍ). The key point is that the renewal is of the same accident individuals, not of new accidents.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage clarifies that it means renewing the same individual accidents (their persons or selves), not new ones or substances.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
An accident is defined as dependent existence, and the preferred view is that the same individuals of accidents are renewed, not new ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that an accident has no independent existence and depends entirely on another for its spatial extension. You are created from an accident in the sense that your very being is dependent on Allah at every moment. Do you feel the weight of that dependency not as a weakness but as the only truth about your existence, and how would your daily actions change if you never forgot it for a single heartbeat?
?
The renewal of the same accident individuals teaches that nothing—not your health, your wealth, your relationships—continues except by Allah's constant re-creation of them. When you received a blessing yesterday, did you truly believe that it could vanish in an instant without His renewal? And if you believed it, why did you not thank Him with the same intensity as if you had been brought back from nothing?
?
If the very individuals of accidents are renewed, then what you call 'yourself'—your body, your mind, your spirit—is renewed every moment by the will of Allah. How would you live today if you knew that each moment you are a fresh creation, with no residue of past sins clinging to you except as Allah wills, and that every breath is a chance to turn to Him completely?

The Recreation of Times: Two Opinions
Definition of zaman and explanation of the two opinions on its recreation with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opinions in Aqeedah regarding the recreation of time in the afterlife, the evidence for the preferred view, and how the objection of logical contradiction is resolved.

Core Idea
Time is recreated gradually with bodies, not all at once.

This lesson explores a fascinating topic from Sam'iyyat: whether the times experienced by bodies in this world are recreated in the afterlife. The author presents two opinions, defends the preponderant view with Quranic and prophetic evidence, and addresses the objection that recreating times would cause logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of zaman
Read the definition of time (zaman) as a known continuity used to measure unknown change. Note the example 'I will come at sunrise' to clarify the concept.
2
Examine the first opinion and its evidence
The first and preponderant opinion holds that all times are recreated with bodies. The evidence includes the Quranic verse about skin replacement (4:56) and the miracle of the sun returning for the Prophet.
Notice that the 'otherness' of skins is with respect to time, not essence.
3
Analyze the second opinion and its refutation
The second opinion denies recreation due to the contradiction of past, present, and future. But the refutation explains that recreation is gradual, not instantaneous, so no contradiction occurs.
The gradual nature is key to resolving the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات
[إعادة أزمنة الأجسام]
١٠٠. وفي الزَّمَنْ قَوْلَانِ والحِسَابُ حَقُّ وَمَا فِي حَقٌّ ارْتِيَابُ
(وَفِي) جواز إعادة (الزَّمَنْ هو مُتجدِّدُ معلوم ، يُقدَّر به مُتجدّد غير معلوم، وهو كقولهم: مُقارنة مُتَجَدِّدٍ موهوم لمُتَجَدِّدٍ معلوم ، إزالة للإبهام، نحو: آتيك عند طلوع الشَّمس (قَوْلَانِ): في أحدهما: - وهو الأرجح - : إعادة جميع أزمنة الأجسام التي مرَّتْ عليها الدُّنيا تبعاً للذوات والأجسام المُعادَة؛ فَتُعاد بأزمِنَتِها وأوقاتِها كما تُعاد بأَكْوَانِها وهَيْئَاتِها؛ لورودِ ظاهر القرآن به في قوله تعالى: ﴿ كُلَّمَا نَضِجَتْ جُلُودُهُم بَدَّلْتَهُمْ جُلُودًا غَيْرَهَا) [النساء: ٥٦] ، لأنَّ المُراد الغيريَّة بحسب الزمان؛ وإلا فالجلود هي الأولى بأعيانها ؛ إذ هي التي عَصتْ ، فيُعاد تأليفها إذا تفرّقت وأعيانها إذا عُدمت ، وقد رُدَّتِ الشَّمسُ بعد غروبها بدعائه . وثانيهما: امتناع إعادتها لاجتماع المتنافيات، كالماضي والحال والاستقبال ، وإِنْ أَجيب عنه : بأنَّ الإعادة ليست دفعيَّة ؛ بل على التدريج حَسْبَ ما كانتْ في الدُّنيا
Section of Sam'iyyat (Revealed Matters). [Recreating the Times of Bodies] 100. And regarding time there are two opinions, and the Reckoning is true, and there is no doubt in the truth. (And regarding) the permissibility of recreating (time – it is a known continuity, by which an unknown continuity is estimated, and it is like their saying: the correlation of an imaginary continuity with a known continuity, to remove ambiguity, like: 'I will come to you at sunrise' – there are two opinions: The first – and it is the more preponderant – is the recreation of all the times of bodies that passed in the world as a consequence of the recreated essences and bodies; so they are recreated with their times and moments just as they are recreated with their states (akwan) and configurations (hay'at); due to the apparent meaning of the Quran indicating it in His statement: 'Every time their skins are cooked We replace them with other skins' [4:56], because the intended otherness is with respect to time; otherwise the skins are the first themselves, for they are the ones that disobeyed, so their composition is re-created when they disintegrate and their essences when they are annihilated, and the sun was returned after its setting by his supplication. And the second: the impossibility of its recreation because it would combine contradictory things, such as past, present, and future. And it is answered: the recreation is not instantaneous; rather, it is gradual according to what it was in the world.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the matn verse on the topics of time and the Reckoning. It begins by defining 'zaman' (time) as a known continuity used to estimate an unknown continuity, clarifying it with an example. Then it presents two opinions regarding the recreating of the times of bodies in the afterlife. The first and preponderant opinion holds that all the times that bodies experienced in this world are recreated along with the bodies themselves, just as their states and configurations are recreated. The evidence is the Quranic verse about skin replacement in Hell, where the 'otherness' of the skins is with respect to time, not essence. Additionally, the miracle of the sun returning after sunset for the Prophet is cited as a precedent. The second opinion denies the possibility of recreating times, arguing that it would require combining past, present, and future simultaneously. The response is that the recreation is not instantaneous but gradual, exactly as the times originally occurred in the world.

Correction: The recreation is gradual, exactly as times originally occurred, so no contradiction occurs.

Key Takeaway

The Definition of al-Hisab (The Reckoning)
Al-Laqani defines al-hisab linguistically and technically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definition of al-hisab (the Reckoning) as given by Al-Laqani, and the context of belief in the Reckoning.

Core Idea
Al-hisab is not just counting; it is God’s personal disclosure to His servants.

This lesson begins the section on belief in the Reckoning (al-Hisab). Al-Laqani starts by defining the term, providing both its everyday meaning and its specific theological sense. You will see how a familiar word takes on a deeper significance in the context of the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
The passage is short. Notice the phrase 'al-Hisab' and its two definitions. The text cuts off, but the definition is clear so far.
The passage is incomplete due to page cut-off, but the key idea is present.
2
Understand the linguistic meaning
Linguistically, al-hisab means 'counting' (al-‘add). This shows that the term originates in a basic human act of enumeration.
3
Grasp the technical definition
Technically, al-hisab is 'God informing His servants' (tawqif). This means the Reckoning is not merely arithmetic; it is a divine act of making known what they have done.
The word 'tawqif' implies a stopping or making aware.

The Reckoning (al-Hisab): Definition and Three Modes
Al-Laqqani defines al-hisab and lists its three possible modes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of al-hisab in Islamic theology and the three ways in which Allah will reckon with His servants on the Day of Judgment.

Core Idea
The reckoning is detailed, not by weight, and occurs through one of three divine modalities.

In this lesson, Al-Laqqani provides a precise definition of al-hisab (the reckoning) and then explains the three possible ways it will occur for different people. Understanding these modalities clarifies the nature of the Final Account and distinguishes it from other concepts like the weighing of deeds. Let's examine the passage to grasp this important eschatological reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-hisab
First, read the Arabic and translation of the definition, noting both the linguistic and technical meanings. Observe that the definition includes details about timing, scope, and exception.
2
Identify the three modalities
The passage then lists three ways the reckoning can occur. List them: (1) necessary knowledge, (2) showing books with forgiveness/multiplication, (3) divine speech. Note the Quranic references implicitly.
The third modality distinguishes between Allah's eternal speech and a sound.
3
Reflect on the exception and purpose
Consider who is excluded from this detailed accounting and why. Think about how these three modalities cover different categories of believers and the mercy involved.
The exception refers to those who enter Paradise without reckoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالحِسَابُ لغةً: العَدُّ، واصطلاحًا: توقيف الله عباده قَبْلَ الانصراف من المحشر على أعمالهم، قولا كانت أو فعلا أو اعتقادا، مكسوبةً أو لا، بعد أخذ كتبها، خيرًا كانت أو شرا، تفصيلا لا بالوزن، إلا من استثني منهم، إما بأن يخلق الله في قلوبهم علوما ضرورية بمقادير أعمالهم من الثواب والعقاب، وإما بأن يوقفهم بين يديه ويؤتيهم كتب أعمالهم فيها سيئاتهم وحسناتهم، فيقول: هذه سيئاتكم وقد تجاوزت عنها، وهذه حسناتكم وقد ضاعفتها لكم، وإما بأن يكلمهم في شأن أعمالهم وكيفية ما لها من الثواب وما عليها من العقاب؛ فيسمعهم كلامه القديم أو صوتا يدل عليه.
And al-hisab linguistically: counting, and technically: Allah's stopping His servants before departing from the gathering place concerning their deeds, whether words or actions or beliefs, acquired or not, after taking their books, whether good or evil, in detail, not by weight, except those who are excluded from them, either by Allah creating in their hearts necessary knowledge of the amounts of their deeds of reward and punishment, or by stopping them before Him and giving them their books of deeds in which are their sins and good deeds, and He says: 'These are your sins and I have overlooked them, and these are your good deeds and I have multiplied them for you,' or by speaking to them concerning their deeds and the manner of what reward they have and what punishment they have, so He makes them hear His eternal speech or a sound indicating it.
Source pages p. 518

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-hisab' (reckoning) and then explains the ways in which it occurs. The author first gives the linguistic meaning as 'counting' and then the technical meaning as Allah's stopping His servants before departing from the gathering place (al-mahshar) concerning their deeds, whether words, actions, or beliefs, whether acquired or not, after the books of deeds have been given, whether good or evil, in detail, not by weight. Then an exception is mentioned: except for those who are excluded from this detailed accounting. The author then enumerates three possible modalities for the reckoning. (1) Allah creates in the hearts of the people necessary knowledge of the amounts of their deeds in terms of reward and punishment, so they know their due without a verbal accounting. (2) Allah stops them before Him and gives them their books containing their sins and good deeds, and He says: These are your sins and I have overlooked them, and these are your good deeds and I have multiplied them for you. (3) Allah speaks to them concerning their deeds and the nature of the reward and punishment they have, making them hear His eternal speech (kalamuhu al-qadim) or a sound that indicates it. These three ways cover the possible forms of the reckoning for different categories of people, with the exception of those who will enter Paradise without any reckoning.

Key Terms
الحساب
التوقيف
المحشر
الكتب
كلامه القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The reckoning is the same as weighing deeds.
Correction: Al-hisab is a detailed accounting, distinct from al-mizan (weighing).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the reckoning (al-hisab) carried out?
A) Only by weighing deeds.
B) By one of three possible modalities: necessary knowledge, presentation of books, or divine speech.
C) By a detailed verbal accounting for everyone without exception.
D) By counting deeds without any verbal interaction.

Key Takeaway
The reckoning occurs in one of three ways: through necessary knowledge, presentation of books with divine declaration, or direct divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three modes of reckoning do you most fear or hope for yourself? What does that reveal about your current relationship with your deeds?
?
If Allah were to give you your book of deeds today and say 'I have overlooked your sins,' what would be your immediate reaction? Would you truly believe in His forgiveness, or would you cling to shame and guilt?
?
The reckoning is in detail, not by weight. Does the thought of every word, action, and belief being individually accounted for fill you with dread or motivate you to change? What one habit will you begin to track and repent from today so it is not exposed then?

The Three Modes of Reckoning
The author enumerates three modes of reckoning on the Day of Judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct ways Allah will reckon with His servants on the Day of Judgment, as described in the Sam‘iyyat section.

Core Idea
Allah's reckoning is not one-size-fits-all; He uses three modes for different people.

This passage from the Sam‘iyyat section explains how Allah will judge His servants on the Day of Judgment. It describes three different methods of reckoning, each tailored to different individuals. The author distinguishes these modes to show the variety of divine justice and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Let the rhythm of the text sink in. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the key term 'سمعيات' which indicates matters known through revelation.
2
Identify the three modes
Find the three phrases introduced by 'إما بأن' (either by...). Each phrase describes a distinct mode of reckoning. Write them down in order.
The modes are: 1) creating necessary knowledge, 2) presenting the book and speaking, 3) speaking directly.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why Allah would use different modes for different people. What does this tell us about His justice and mercy? How does the second mode demonstrate both forgiveness and multiplication of rewards?
This passage shows that Allah tailors His judgment to each person's state and need.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ستثني منهم قسم السمعيات الانصراف مِن المَحْشَر على أعمالهم، قولا كانت أو فعلا أو اعتقادا، مكسوبةً أو لا، بعد أَخْذِ كتبها، خيرًا كانت أو شرا، تفصيلا لا بالوزن، إِلَّا مَن، إما بأن يخلق الله فى قلوبهم علوماً ضرورية بمقادير أعمالهم من الثواب والعقاب، وإما بأن يُوقِفهم بين يديه ويؤتيهم كُتُبَ أعمالهم فيها سيِّئاتهم وحسناتهم، فيقول: هذه سيئاتكم وقد تجاوزت عنها، وهذه حسناتكم وقد ضاعفتُها لكم، وإما بأنْ يُكلِّمهم فى شأن أعمالهم وكيفية ما لها من الثَّواب وما عليها من العِقاب؛ فيُسمعهم كلامَهُ القديمَ أو صوتا يدلُّ عليه.
And they are excepted from them: the section of sam‘iyyat (audible matters), the departure from the gathering place according to their deeds, whether speech, action, or belief, acquired or not, after taking their books, whether good or evil, in detail, not by weight, except for one, either by God creating in their hearts necessary knowledges of the amounts of their deeds of reward and punishment, or by Him stopping them before Him and giving them their books of deeds containing their evil deeds and good deeds, saying: 'These are your evil deeds and I have overlooked them, and these are your good deeds and I have multiplied them for you,' or by Him speaking to them concerning their deeds and the nature of what reward they have and what punishment they incur, making them hear His eternal speech or a sound indicating it.
Source pages p. 519

Commentary
This passage enumerates three modes of the reckoning (al-hisab) on the Day of Judgment, specifically for those who are excepted from general reckoning. The author first states that the discussion falls under sam‘iyyat (matters known through revelation). He then describes the departure from the gathering place based on deeds, detailed rather than weighed. Then three modes are listed: first, God creates necessary knowledge in the hearts of the amounts of reward and punishment; second, God stops them and gives them their books, forgiving evil deeds and multiplying good deeds; third, God speaks to them directly, making them hear His eternal speech or a sound indicating it. The word 'except' indicates that these modes apply to some people, not all.

The Nature of the Reckoning and Its Modalities
Sanusi defines the reckoning and lists its varying modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the reckoning is defined in Sunni belief, its creation by God, and the different ways it can occur.

Core Idea
The reckoning is a real event created by God, unique to each person, and varies in difficulty and manner.

This passage from al-Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid establishes the foundational belief in the reckoning (al-hisab). It clarifies that God creates a sound specifically for each responsible being, allowing them to hear their account alone, and that the reckoning can take various forms. Let's explore the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify how the reckoning is created
The passage says God creates a sound in the ear of each mukallaf or near it. Understand that this ensures privacy of the reckoning.
3
Reflect on the modalities
The passage lists six modalities: easy/difficult, secret/public, rebuke/grace/justice. Consider why these variations are important.
This shows the reckoning is not one-size-fits-all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالحساب يخلقه سبحانه في أُذُنِ كلَّ واحدٍ مِن المُكلَّفين، أو في محلّ يَقْرُبُ مِن أُذُنِه بحيث لا تبلغ قوَّةُ ذلك الصوت منعَ الغير من سماع ما كُلف به، وهذا هو الذي تشهد له الأحاديثُ الصَّحيحةُ وتَتَّسع قدرته لمحاسبتهم معاً، كما تتسع لإحداثهم معا، وكيفيته مختلفةٌ، فمنه: اليسير والعسير، والسِّرُّ والجهر، والتوبيخ، والفضل والعدل، ويكون للمؤمن والكافر إنسا وجنَّا، إلَّا مَن ورَدَ الحديث باستثنائهم، كالسَّبعين ألفًا، وأفضلهم أبو بكر الصديق، فلا يُحاسب؛ لما روي عن عائشة: «النَّاس كلهم يحاسبون إلَّا أبا بكر» وأوَّلُ مَن
Belief in the reckoning: He (God) creates it in the ear of every one of the responsible beings, or in a place near his ear such that the strength of that sound does not prevent others from hearing what he is charged with, and this is what the authentic hadiths testify to. And His power encompasses reckoning them all together, just as it encompasses creating them together. And its modality varies: among it are the easy and the difficult, the secret and the public, the rebuke, and the grace and justice. And it is for the believer and the disbeliever, humans and jinn, except for those whom the hadith excepts, such as the seventy thousand, and the best of them is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, so he is not reckoned, because of what is narrated from ‘Aisha: 'All people will be reckoned except Abu Bakr.' And the first one...
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage defines the belief in the reckoning (al-hisab) and elaborates its details. It states that God creates a sound in the ear of each morally responsible being (mukallaf) or near it, so that they alone hear their charge. Authentic hadiths confirm this. God’s power encompasses reckoning all at once, just as He creates them all at once. The reckoning varies: it can be easy or difficult, secret or public, involves rebuke, grace, or justice. It applies to believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn, except those exempted by hadith, such as the seventy thousand who enter Paradise without reckoning, and the best among them is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, who is not reckoned based on a hadith from ‘Aisha. The passage ends mid-sentence but the key points are covered.

المكلَّفين

Key Takeaway
The reckoning is a real, individualized event created by God, with varying modalities.

The Exceptions to the Reckoning: The Seventy Thousand and Abu Bakr
Sanusi explains the universality of the reckoning and its exceptions based on hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the reckoning applies to all responsible beings except those explicitly exempted by hadith, such as the seventy thousand and Abu Bakr al-Siddiq.

Core Idea
The reckoning is universal for all mukallaf, but some are exempt by divine grace as specified in hadith.

The preceding lesson established the nature of the reckoning. This lesson focuses on its scope: everyone will be reckoned — believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn — with exceptions for a special group mentioned in hadith, including the seventy thousand who enter Paradise without reckoning and the exemplary Abu Bakr al-Siddiq.

The Modality and Types of Divine Reckoning
Sanusi explains the modality, types, and exceptions of divine reckoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah creates the reckoning for each individual, the various types it takes, the exceptions, and who is the first to be reckoned from this community.

Core Idea
Allah creates the reckoning sound individually for each person, and its types vary from easy to difficult, secret to public.

This passage from Sanusi explains the modality of the divine reckoning (al-hisab). It affirms that Allah creates a sound directly in each accountable person's ear, and His power is vast enough to reckon all simultaneously. The reckoning takes different forms depending on the person's deeds—easy or difficult, secret or public, reproachful or by favor and justice. There are exceptions for certain righteous individuals, like Abu Bakr, and the first to be reckoned from this community is the Prophet Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Understand the modality of reckoning
Read how Allah creates the sound of reckoning directly in each person's ear or near it, so that others are not disturbed.
This emphasizes individual accountability.
2
Recognize the types of reckoning
Identify the pairs: easy/difficult, secret/public, reproachful/favor and justice.
These types show that reckoning is not only punitive.
3
Note the exceptions and sequence
Learn which groups are exempted (e.g., seventy thousand, Abu Bakr) and that the Prophet is the first to be reckoned.
These exceptions highlight divine mercy and honor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالحساب يخلقه سبحانه في أُذُنِ كلَّ واحدٍ مِن المُكلَّفين، أو في محلّ يَقْرُبُ مِن أُذُنِه بحيث لا تبلغ قوَّةُ ذلك الصوت منعَ الغير من سماع ما كلف به، وهذا هو الذي تشهد له الأحاديثُ الصَّحيحةُ (۱) وتَتَّسع قدرته (۲) لمحاسبتهم معاً، كما تتسع لإحداثهم معا، وكيفيته مختلفةٌ، فمنه: اليسير والعسير، والسِّرُّ والجهر، والتوبيخ، والفضل والعدل، ويكون للمؤمن والكافر إنسا وجنَّا، إلَّا مَن ورَدَ الحديث باستثنائهم، كالسَّبعين ألفًا، وأفضلهم أبو بكر الصديق، فلا يُحاسب؛ لما روي عن عائشة: «النَّاس كلهم يحاسبون إلَّا أبا بكر»(۳)، وأوَّلُ مَن يحاسَبُ هذه الأُمَّهُ (۱)
Belief in the Reckoning: He, exalted be He, creates it in the ear of every one of the morally responsible (mukallaf), or in a place near his ear such that the power of that sound does not prevent others from hearing what he is charged with. This is what the authentic hadiths attest to. And His power encompasses reckoning them all together, just as it encompasses creating them all together. Its modality varies: some is easy and some difficult, some secret and some public, some reproachful and some [by] favor and justice. It applies to the believer and the disbeliever, human and jinn, except those for whom a hadith has come exempting them, such as the seventy thousand, and the best of them is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq—he will not be reckoned, because of what was narrated from Aisha: 'All people will be reckoned except Abu Bakr.' And the first to be reckoned of this community.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage defines the modality and scope of the divine Reckoning (al-Hisab). It begins by stating that belief in the Reckoning is required. Then it explains that Allah creates a sound directly in each accountable person's ear or close to it, and this sound is specifically directed so that only that person hears it, while others are not prevented from hearing their own. The passage affirms that this is supported by authentic hadiths. Next, it emphasizes that Allah's power is vast enough to reckon all creatures simultaneously, just as He creates them all at once. The types of reckoning are then listed: easy and difficult, secret and public, reproachful and (by) favor and justice. It applies to both believers and disbelievers, humans and jinn, with the exception of those specifically exempted by hadith, such as the seventy thousand who enter Paradise without reckoning, and among them the best is Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, who will not be reckoned based on a hadith from Aisha. Finally, the passage notes that the first to be reckoned from this community will be the Prophet Muhammad, and that this belief is obligatory.

Key Takeaway

Proof and Wisdom of Reckoning
Sanusi proves reckoning from revelation and explains its wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the reckoning is established by revelation and rational principle, and that its wisdom is to manifest degrees of perfection and deter evil.

Core Idea
The reckoning is a certain truth from revelation with a clear divine purpose.

Sanusi now turns to one of the essential sam'iyyat matters: the reckoning. He first proves its reality from the three sources of Islamic knowledge, then applies a rational principle that whatever the truthful Prophet informs about must occur. Finally, he explains the divine wisdom behind it, showing that the reckoning is not arbitrary but serves to manifest justice and encourage righteousness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's flow.
Notice the compact style: proof first, then wisdom, then a concluding remark.
2
Identify the three proofs
The passage cites the Quran, Sunnah, and ijma' as evidence for the reckoning. Locate each citation.
The Quranic verse is 'Swift in reckoning' and the hadith is 'Reckon yourselves before you are reckoned.'
3
Understand the rational principle
Sanusi argues that the reckoning is a possible matter (mumkin) that the Truthful (al-Sadiq) informed about, so it must be actual. Reflect on why this makes belief obligatory.
This principle applies to all sam'iyyat matters.
4
Grasp the wisdom
The wisdom has two aspects: manifesting differences in perfection and increasing pleasures/pains to encourage good and deter evil. Consider how this fits divine justice.
The reckoning is not punishment for its own sake but a tool for moral growth.
5
Reflect on the concluding warning
Sanusi ends by saying that a believer should not act like a denier. How does this reinforce the lesson?
Belief in the reckoning should transform one's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات (حَقٌّ) ، أي : ثابت بالكتاب والسُّنَّة والإجماع ؛ ففي القرآن: سَرِيعُ الْحِسَابِ [إبراهيم: ٥١]، وفي السُّنَّة حاسبوا أنفسكم قَبْلَ أَن تحاسبوا»(۲) وأجمع المسلمون عليه ، وهو من الأمور الممكنة التي أخبر بها الصَّادق ، وكلُّ ما هو كذلك فهو واقع ، والإيمان به واجب. وحكمته إظهار تفاوت المراتب في الكمال، وفضائح أصحاب النقص، زيادة في اللذات والآلام ؛ ففيه ترغيب في الحسنات وزجر عن السَّيِّئات (وَمَا فِي) وقوع (حَقِّ ارْتِيَابُ) أي: شكٍّ ، فمَن صدَّق به لا ينبغي أن يصدر عنه ما يصدر عن نَافِيه(۳)
The section of sam'iyyat (matters heard from revelation) — (it is true), i.e., established by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus (ijma'). For in the Qur'an: 'Swift in reckoning' [Ibrahim: 51], and in the Sunnah: 'Reckon yourselves before you are reckoned,' and the Muslims have agreed upon it. It is among possible matters (al-umur al-mumkina) about which the Truthful One (al-Sadiq, i.e., the Prophet) informed, and everything that is so is actual. Belief in it is obligatory. Its wisdom is to manifest the difference in ranks of perfection, and the scandals of the deficient, increasing pleasures and pains; for in it is encouragement towards good deeds and deterrence from evil deeds. And there is no doubt in its occurrence. So whoever believes in it should not do what is done by one who denies it.
Source pages p. 521

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof and wisdom section for the reality of the Reckoning. It begins by categorizing this topic under 'al-sam'iyyat' (matters known from revelation). The statement '(haqq)' means it is true, established by three sources: the Qur'an (citing 'Swift in reckoning'), the Sunnah (citing the command 'Reckon yourselves before you are reckoned'), and the consensus of Muslims. It then applies a rational principle: the Reckoning is a possible matter (mumkin) that the Truthful Prophet informed of, and everything possible that the truthful informs about must be actual; thus belief is obligatory. Next it explains the divine wisdom behind the Reckoning: to manifest the different degrees of perfection among souls, to expose the shortcomings of the deficient, and to increase pleasures for the obedient and pains for the disobedient, thereby encouraging good and deterring evil. The passage concludes by asserting that there is no doubt in its occurrence, and that a true believer should not behave like one who denies it.

The Recompense of Evil and Good Deeds
Al-Bajuri defines sayyi'at and hasanat and explains their recompense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic doctrine regarding the recompense of evil deeds and good deeds on the Day of Judgment, as defined by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Evil deeds are repaid by the like; good deeds are multiplied by divine grace.

In this passage, al-Bajuri unpacks the meaning of a key verse from the Jawhara about the reckoning. He defines what constitutes an evil deed (sayyi'ah) and a good deed (hasanah) according to the Law, and then explains the divine rule for their recompense. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Islamic theology of reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the explanation of evil deeds
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the verse sets up the two categories.
2
Understand the definition of sayyi'at and the rule 'by the like'
The text defines evil deeds as blameworthy acts, then states their recompense is equivalent to the sin, with the possibility of pardon except for disbelief.
The term 'by the like' implies exact proportionality.
3
Understand the definition of hasanat and the rule 'multiplied by grace'
Good deeds are praiseworthy acts, and they are rewarded multiple times by God's grace without limit.
Multiplication is a gift, not an obligation.
4
Reflect on the asymmetry: justice vs. generosity
Evil deeds are treated with strict justice (like for like), while good deeds are treated with generosity (multiplied). This highlights God's mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإيمان بالحساب
١٠١- فالسَّيِّئَاتُ عِنْدَهُ بِالْمِثْلِ وَالحَسَنَاتُ ضُوعِفَتْ بالفَضْل
(فَالسَّيِّئاتُ) وهي : ما يُذمَّ فاعله شرعاً ، والمراد: التي عملها العبد حقيقةً أو حكمًا، بأن طُرحت عليه لظلامته الغير ونفاد حسناته، صغيرة كانت أو كبيرة، جزاؤها (عِنْدَهُ تعالى (بِالمِثْلِ)، أي: مُقَدَّرُ بمثلها سواء بسواء ، إن جازاه الله تعالى عليها ، وله أن يعفو عنها إن لم تكن كُفْرًا ، وسُمِّيت سيّئةً لأنَّ فاعِلها يُسَاء بها عند المقابلة عليها.
(وَالحَسَنَاتُ) هي : ما يُحمَدُ فاعِلُه شرعًا، لحُسن وجه صاحبها عند رؤيتها ، والمُراد : الحسنات المقبولة الأصليّة المَعمُولة لهم أو في حكمها، لا المأخوذة في نظير ظلاماتهم (ضُوعِفَتْ) أي: ضاعفها الله تعالى لهذه الأمة وكَثَّرَ ثوابَها إلى مثلها أو أكثر من غير انتهاء إلى حد تقِفُ عندَه بِالفَضْل) أي : بفضله تعالى وكَرَمِه، وهو: العطاء لا عن وجوب ولا عن إيجاب عليه سبحانه .
Belief in the Reckoning
101 — So evil deeds with Him are by the like, and good deeds are multiplied by grace.
(Evil deeds) are: that which the doer is blameworthy for according to the Law, and the intended [meaning]: those which the servant performed in reality or by ruling, by being imposed upon him for his transgression against another and the exhaustion of his good deeds, whether small or great. Their recompense with Him, exalted, is by the like, i.e., measured by the equivalent exactly, if God, exalted, recompenses him for them, and He may pardon them if they are not disbelief. They are called 'sayyi'ah' because the doer is grieved by them when subjected to recompense.
(Good deeds) are: that which the doer is praised for according to the Law, due to the good appearance of their owner when seen. And the intended [meaning]: the accepted original good deeds performed by them or in the same ruling, not those taken in exchange for their transgressions. (They are multiplied) i.e., God, exalted, multiplies them for this nation and increases their reward up to the like of them or more, without ending at a limit that stops there, (by grace) i.e., by His grace and generosity, and it is giving without obligation and without compulsion upon Him, exalted.
Source pages p. 522

Commentary
This passage defines the two key terms in the verse line—'sayyi'at' (evil deeds) and 'hasanat' (good deeds)—and explains the rule of recompense for each in the Divine reckoning. 

First, the author defines 'sayyi'at': they are actions for which the doer is blameworthy according to the Sacred Law. The intended meaning is those deeds that the servant actually performed ('ḥaqīqatan') or is legally considered to have performed ('ḥukman'), such as when they are imposed on a wrongdoer due to his oppression of others and the depletion of his own good deeds. These may be minor or major sins. Their recompense with God is 'by the like' — that is, if God chooses to punish them, the punishment is exactly equivalent to the sin. However, God may pardon them, unless the sin is disbelief. They are called 'sayyi'ah' because the doer will be grieved when facing the recompense. 

Second, the author defines 'hasanat': they are actions for which the doer is praised according to the Law, because the good appearance of their owner is pleasing to see. The intended meaning is the accepted, original good deeds that the servant actually performed or deeds that are in the same ruling (e.g., charity given on one's behalf). This excludes good deeds that were taken in exchange for wrongs (i.e., taken from the wrongdoer). As for their recompense, God multiplies them for this Muslim nation, increasing their reward to the equivalent or more, without any upper limit. This multiplication is 'by grace' — a gift from God without any obligation or compulsion upon Him. 

The passage thus clarifies the fundamental asymmetry: sins are punished by the like, but good deeds are multiplied by God's free generosity.

Key Terms
سيئات (sayyi'at)
حسنات (hasanat)
المثل (al-mithl)
الفضل (al-fadl)
حقيقة (haqiqah)
حكمًا (hukman)
مضاعفة (muda'afah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that good deeds are also recompensed strictly by the like, but the text clarifies they are multiplied by grace.
Correction: Good deeds are multiplied by God's grace, not repaid by the like as evil deeds are.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rule for recompense of evil deeds (sayyi'at)?
A) They are multiplied by grace like good deeds.
B) They are recompensed by the like, meaning exactly equivalent, but God may pardon them if not disbelief.
C) They are always punished with more than the like.
D) They are forgiven unconditionally.

Key Takeaway
Evil deeds are punished by the like, but good deeds are multiplied by God's grace without limit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have done many good deeds—how many of them are truly 'accepted original good deeds' free from taint of wronging others? Think of one specific good deed today: was there someone you harmed that you haven't made things right with? Will you seek their forgiveness now?
?
This lesson teaches that evil deeds are exactly recompensed unless God pardons. When you sin, do you truly consider the weight of that exact recompense, or do you assume God will simply overlook it? What sin do you keep repeating, and how does this lesson change your resolve to abandon it?
?
Good deeds are multiplied by grace, not obligation. How does that truth strike your heart—does it drive you to humble gratitude or to lazy presumption? If God's grace is the only reason your good deeds multiply, are you living as if you depend entirely on His generosity, or are you secretly proud of your own efforts?

The Multiplication of Good Deeds: Definition and Scope
Definition of tad'if and explanation of its nature and scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of tad'if (multiplication) of good deeds, its source as God's grace, and its limitation to actually performed deeds.

Core Idea
Tad'if is God's unlimited multiplication of reward for performed good deeds, purely by grace.

The commentator explains what is meant by 'multiplied' in the poem. He clarifies that Allah multiplies the reward for the believers' good deeds without limit, and that this multiplication is entirely an act of grace, not an obligation. He also distinguishes between performed deeds (which are multiplied) and mere intentions or abandoned sins (which are not multiplied). This lesson unpacks these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the key terms used.
2
Understand the definition
The passage defines tad'if as multiplication by Allah, increasing reward to its like or more with no limit.
This multiplication is a grace from Allah, not due to any obligation.
3
Identify exceptions
Find the two exceptions mentioned: good deeds from refraining from sin (like abandoning adultery) and good deeds intended but not performed.
These are not multiplied, only written as a single good deed.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why multiplication applies only to performed deeds: it emphasizes that actual obedience is the basis for multiplied reward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضُوعِفَتْ أي: ضاعفها الله تعالى لهذه الأمة وكَثَّرَ ثوابَها إلى مثلها أو أكثر من غير انتهاء إلى حد تقِفُ عندَه بِالفَضْل أي : بفضله تعالى وكَرَمِه، وهو: العطاء لا عن وجوب ولا عن إيجاب عليه سبحانه . لا الحاصلة بالتضعيف . وقوله : (المعمولة) خرج ما إذا كان هم على الزنا ثم تركه ; فله حسنة من غير مضاعفة , وكذلك الحسنة التي هم بها فتكتب واحدة من غير تضعيف.
It is multiplied: i.e., Allah the Exalted multiplied them for this ummah and increased their reward to its like or more, without end at a limit at which it stops, by grace: i.e., by His grace and generosity, and it is giving not from obligation nor from compulsion upon Him. Not the one obtained by multiplication. And His saying: (the performed) excludes the case when one intended adultery and then left it; for him is a good deed without multiplication, and likewise the good deed that one intends is written as one without multiplication.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'tad'if' (multiplication) as it appears in the poem. The commentator explains that 'multiplied' means Allah multiplies the reward for this ummah, increasing it to an amount equal to the original or more, with no upper limit. This multiplication is by God's grace and generosity, not due to any obligation or compulsion upon Him. Then the commentator distinguishes between the original reward and the multiplied reward: the original reward is not the result of multiplication. He also clarifies that a good deed that is intended but not performed, like intending adultery then refraining, is written as a single good deed without multiplication. Similarly, a good deed merely intended is written as one, not multiplied. Thus, multiplication applies only to actually performed good deeds.

The Rule of Multiplication: Sinners and Disbelievers
The commentator explains the divine rule of reward and punishment with scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic creed regarding the multiplication of rewards for good deeds, specifically the conditions under which it applies to sinners and why it does not apply to disbelievers.

Core Idea
Multiplication of reward applies to accepted good deeds of believers, not to disbelievers.

This lesson addresses a key creedal point about divine justice and mercy. The commentator explains what must be believed regarding the recompense of good and bad deeds. He clarifies that while good deeds are multiplied, the degree depends on sincerity. A crucial distinction is made: multiplication applies to the good deeds of sinful believers but not to disbelievers, because disbelief invalidates the acceptance of any act of obedience.

Guided Path
1
Understand the foundational rule
Read the first part of the passage: bad deeds are recompensed with an equivalent, good deeds are multiplied. This is the basic principle.
The rule is derived from the Qur'anic verse cited.
2
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
The commentator quotes Surah al-An'am (6:160) to support the rule. Notice how the verse speaks of ten times for a good deed and an equal recompense for a bad deed.
3
Recognize the variation due to sincerity
The passage explains that the degree of multiplication varies according to the sincerity and good intention accompanying the good deed. Reflect on why this makes sense in Islamic theology.
4
Grasp the correct view on sinners
The commentator states that multiplication enters into the good deeds of sinners (believers who commit sins) provided those deeds are performed in a manner that allows for acceptance and approval. Understand that this excludes hypocrisy or show.
5
Understand why disbelievers are excluded
The passage clearly says that multiplication does not apply to disbelievers because an accepted obedience cannot coexist with disbelief. This is a key theological distinction.
The commentator also clarifies that multiplication is specific to the original reward, not the reward from multiplication itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومراد الناظم: أنَّ ممَّا يجب اعتقاده: مقابلةُ السَّيِّئة بمثلها إن قوبلت، ومقابلة الحسنة بضعفها , قال تعالى : {وَمَن جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا} [الأنعام: ١٦٠]، وتفاوتُ مراتبِ التَّضعيف بحسب ما يقترن بالحسنة من الإخلاص وحُسن النية. والصَّواب: دخول المضاعفة حسنات العُصاة، إن كانت على وجه يتناوله القبول والرّضا، وعدم دخولها في أعمال الكُفَّار ; لأنَّه لا يجتمع مع الكفرِ طاعة مقبولة، وهو خاص بالثّوابِ الأصلي دون الحاصل بالتضعيف.
And the intent of the versifier is that part of what must be believed is: the recompense of a bad deed is its like, if it is recompensed, and the recompense of a good deed is its multiple. Allah the Exalted said: {Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times its like, and whoever comes with a bad deed will not be recompensed except with its like} [Al-An'am 6:160]. And the degrees of multiplication vary according to what accompanies the good deed of sincerity and good intention. And the correct view is that multiplication enters into the good deeds of sinners, if they are in a manner that includes acceptance and approval, and that it does not enter into the deeds of disbelievers, because an accepted obedience does not coexist with disbelief, and it (multiplication) is specific to the original reward, not the one obtained through multiplication.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage presents a creedal point about divine justice and grace. The commentator states that it is obligatory to believe that a bad deed is recompensed with an equivalent punishment, while a good deed is rewarded with multiple times its merit, citing the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-An'am. Then he explains that the degree of multiplication varies according to the sincerity and good intention accompanying the good deed. The correct scholarly position is that multiplication applies to the good deeds of sinners (believers who commit sins), as long as those deeds are performed in a manner that allows for acceptance and approval (i.e., not tainted by hypocrisy or show). However, multiplication does not enter into the deeds of disbelievers, because true obedience cannot coexist with disbelief. Finally, the commentator clarifies that this multiplication is specific to the original reward (the baseline reward for the deed) and not to the reward that comes from multiplication itself—meaning the 'tad'if' is an increase separate from the basic reward.

مضاعفة
إخلاص
كفار
عصاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiplication only applies to sinless believers.
Correction: It also applies to sinners' good deeds if they are accepted, but not to disbelievers.

Checkpoint

A) Because they do not perform any good deeds.
B) Because disbelief prevents the acceptance of any obedience.
C) Because their deeds are only rewarded in this world.
D) Because they are not intended for multiplication.

Key Takeaway
The rule of multiplication applies to accepted good deeds of believing sinners, but not to disbelievers because disbelief nullifies acceptance.

Expiation of Sins: Avoiding Major Sins and Wudu
The commentator explains that minor sins are forgiven by abstaining from major sins, and wudu expiates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins and through acts like wudu, and that the poetic wording does not imply exclusivity.

Core Idea
Avoiding major sins forgives minor sins, and wudu expiates—but these are not the only ways.

This lesson examines a concise verse from a theological poem that identifies two means of forgiveness: abstaining from major sins and performing wudu. The accompanying commentary clarifies a subtle grammatical point, ensuring we do not mistakenly think that avoiding major sins is the exclusive cause. By understanding this, we appreciate the broader scope of divine mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic verse slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the two causes of forgiveness mentioned.
The verse is line 102 of the poem.
2
Identify the two causes
The verse states (1) minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins, and (2) wudu expiates. Recognize these as independent means.
The word 'and' (وَ) joins them.
3
Understand the grammatical nuance
The commentator explains that 'by avoiding' is placed at the beginning for poetic meter, not to indicate exclusivity. This means other causes are possible.
The fronting is for النظم (poetic necessity) لا للاختصاص (not for specification).
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Contemplate how this verse teaches both personal responsibility (avoiding major sins) and reliance on Allah's grace through acts like wudu.
Wudu itself is a means of expiation, as known from hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفَّارَاتُ الذُّنوبِ وأَسبَابُ المَغفِرة] ١٠٢- وَبِاجْتِنَابِ لِلْكَبَائِرٌ تُغْفَرُ صَغَائِرٌ وَجَــا الوُضُو يُكَفِّرُ . قوله: (وباجتناب) متعلق بـ (تغفر) قدمه لضرورة النظم لا للاختصاص، واجتناب الشيء المباعدة عنه . الشنواني .
[Expiation of sins and causes of forgiveness] 102. And by avoiding major sins, minor sins are forgiven, and wudu came as an expiation. His saying: (And by avoiding) is attached to 'are forgiven' – it is placed forward due to poetic necessity, not for specification, and 'avoiding' a thing means distancing oneself from it. Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This is a short lesson covering a line from the poetic text and its gloss. The versifier states that minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins, and that wudu (ablution) also serves as an expiation (kafara) for sins. The commentator clarifies the grammatical point: the word 'by avoiding' (وباجتناب) is fronted because of poetic meter, not to indicate exclusivity—meaning it is not saying that only by avoiding major sins are minor sins forgiven; there are other ways. Then he defines 'avoiding' (اجتناب) as distancing oneself from the thing. The marginal note attributes this to Al-Shanwani. The passage asserts a basic Islamic belief: major sins, if avoided, lead to forgiveness of minor sins, and the act of wudu itself is a cause of expiation.

Correction: The commentator clarifies that the word order is for poetic meter, not to indicate exclusivity. Other causes like wudu also expiate sins.

Checkpoint

B) For poetic necessity, not for specification.
C) To emphasize the importance of avoiding major sins.

Key Takeaway
Minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins, and acts like wudu also expiate sins; the poetic order does not imply exclusivity.

Definition of Major Sin and the Meaning of Avoidance
Al-Amir defines major sin and clarifies the meaning of avoiding it including repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a major sin in Islamic theology and the crucial role of repentance in the concept of avoiding major sins.

Core Idea
Avoidance of major sins includes repentance; it's not about perfection but returning to Allah.

Al-Amir begins by defining what constitutes a major sin (kabirah) — a transgression that shows disregard for religion. He then clarifies that 'avoidance' (ijtinab) of such sins does not mean one must never commit them; rather, it encompasses repenting after falling into them. This nuanced understanding shapes how minor sins are forgiven in relation to major ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the precise wording, especially the definitions and examples.
The passage is from Al-Amir's commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid.
2
Identify the definition of major sin
Find the sentence that defines kabirah. Note the two criteria: the magnitude of punishment and the greatness of the One disobeyed, and the indicator of lack of concern for religion.
The definition is: 'every act of disobedience that indicates the lack of concern of its perpetrator for religion and the thinness of piety.'
3
Understand the two aspects of ijtinab
The author explains that 'avoidance' includes both abstaining from sins and repenting from them if committed. Distinguish between 'never committing' and 'repenting after committing'.
4
Examine the example about minor sins
See how avoidance of major sins forgives minor sins, whether they are preludes or not. Analyze the examples of kiss/touch/look for fornication and insult without penalty.
This shows the mercy of Allah for those who avoid major sins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات. للكبائر، أي: الذنوب العظيمة من حيث المؤاخذة بها وعظمة من عصي بها، وهي: كل معصية تشعر بقلة اكتراث مرتكبها بالدين ورقة الديانة. والمراد من الاجتناب: ما يعم التوبة منها بعد ملابستها، لا ما يخص عدم مقارفتها بالمرة. وأما اجتنابها بعد التلبس بها من غير توبة.. فلا تغفر به ذنوب صغائر بالنسبة لتلك الكبائر من حيث هي صغائر، كانت مقدمات للكبائر المجتنبة كالقبلة واللمس والنظر للزنا، أو لم تكن كشتم بما لا يوجب حدا، إذا اجتنب السرقة والزنا.
Section of the transmitted matters. Concerning major sins, i.e., the great sins from the perspective of punishment and the greatness of the One disobeyed, and they are: every act of disobedience that indicates the lack of concern of its perpetrator for religion and the thinness of piety. What is meant by avoidance: that which includes repentance from them after committing them, not that which is specific to never committing them at all. As for avoiding them after being engaged in them without repentance, then minor sins are not forgiven by it relative to those major sins insofar as they are minor, whether they were preludes to the avoided major sins like the kiss, touch, and look for fornication, or not, like insulting something that does not necessitate a legal penalty, when one avoids theft and fornication.
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage defines 'kabirah' (major sin) and explains 'ijtinab' (avoidance). The author first defines a major sin as a great transgression, emphasizing the magnitude of punishment and the greatness of the One disobeyed. It is any act of disobedience that shows a lack of concern for religion. Then, the author clarifies that 'avoidance' of major sins includes two aspects: first, avoiding them entirely, and second, if one falls into them, repentance is required. The phrase 'avoidance' here does not mean one must never have committed them; rather, it includes repentance after committing. If one engages in a major sin and does not repent, then the avoidance of other major sins does not expiate that sin. However, if one avoids major sins, the minor sins that are not preludes to those major ones are forgiven. For example, an insult that does not warrant a legal penalty is forgiven if one avoids theft and fornication. The passage thus clarifies the nuanced relationship between major and minor sins and the role of repentance.

Key Terms

الصغائر
الاجتناب
التوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that avoidance of major sins means one must never have committed them at all, leaving no room for repentance.
Correction: The text clarifies that avoidance includes repentance after committing; it is not limited to never having committed the sin.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what does 'avoidance of major sins' (ijtinab) primarily include?
A) Never committing any major sin at all.
B) Repenting after committing them if they occur.
C) Only avoiding sin in the future without concern for past.
D) Avoiding all minor sins as well.

Key Takeaway
Major sins are acts that show disregard for religion, and avoiding them includes repentance; this opens the door for minor sins to be forgiven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
