?
The passage defines a major sin as one that shows you don't care enough about your religion. Look at your life: is there a sin you continue in because you've normalized it, not realizing it reveals a lack of concern? What would it mean to treat it as a major sin and repent?

Definition of Forgiveness and Agreement on Expiation
Al-Amir defines forgiveness and notes agreement on expiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of forgiveness (ghufran) in Islamic theology and the agreement among scholars that avoiding major sins leads to expiation of minor sins.

Core Idea
Forgiveness means covering sins; expiation for minor sins is agreed upon, but its certainty is disputed.

This lesson begins by establishing the central concept of forgiveness (ghufran) and the agreed-upon principle that avoiding major sins results in the expiation (takfir) of minor sins. Understanding this foundational agreement sets the stage for the disagreement that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of forgiveness
Read the Arabic definition of ghufran: covering sin through repentance or divine pardon.
2
Identify the agreement
Note that all scholars agree that expiation (takfir) occurs upon avoiding major sins.
This is the common ground.
3
Prepare for the disagreement
Recognize that the next lesson will explore the dispute over whether this forgiveness is certain or probable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفَّارَاتُ الذُّنوبِ وأَسبَابُ المَغفِرة. وغَفْرُ الذَّنب : سَتْرُه بالتّوبة منه ، أو بالعفو ومحو أثره وأمن عاقبته. يعني أنَّ هذا الحكم اختُلِفَ في قطعيته وظنيته، مع الاتفاق على تَرتُبِ التكفير على الاجتناب، فذهب أئمة الكلام إلى أنَّه لا يجب التكفير على القطع؛ بل يجوزُ، ويغلب على الظَّن ويقوى فيها الرَّجاء؛ لأنَّا لو قطعنا المُجتنب الكبائر بتكفير صغائره بالاجتناب؛ لكانت له في حكم المُباح الَّذي يُقطع بأنَّه لا تِباعَةَ فيه، وذلك نقض لعرى الشريعة، فقوله تعالى: إن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُم سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ، معناه : إِنْ شِئنا، حملا له على قوله: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ، وَيَغْفِرُ
Expiations of sins and causes of forgiveness. And forgiveness of sin: covering it through repentance from it, or through pardon and erasing its trace and security from its consequence. Meaning that this ruling differed regarding its certainty and probability, with agreement on the occurrence of expiation upon avoidance. So the imams of kalam held that expiation is not necessarily certain; rather it is permissible, and it predominates in probability and hope is strong in it, because if we were certain that the one who avoids major sins has his minor sins expiated by avoidance, then those sins would be in his case like the permissible which is certain to have no consequence, and that would be a breaking of the bonds of the Sharia. Thus His statement (Exalted): 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' means: 'if We will,' interpreting it by His statement: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, and He forgives'
Source pages p. 525

Commentary
This passage discusses the disagreement regarding the nature of forgiveness of minor sins through avoidance of major sins. The author begins by defining 'ghufran' (forgiveness) as covering the sin through repentance or through divine pardon that erases its effect and ensures safety from its punishment. Then he notes that scholars agree that expiation (takfir) occurs upon avoidance, but they differ on whether this is certain (qat'i) or probable (dhanni). The kalam theologians hold that it is not certain but probable, meaning that one can have strong hope but not absolute certainty. The reasoning is that if one were certain that avoiding major sins automatically expiates all minor sins, then sin would become like permissible actions with no consequences, weakening the fabric of Sharia. Therefore, the Quranic verse (an-Nisa 31) is interpreted as conditional: 'if We will,' analogously to the verse 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills.' Thus forgiveness is subject to divine will and not automatic.

Key Terms
غفران
تكفير
اجتناب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think forgiveness is only through repentance.
Correction: The text defines forgiveness also through divine pardon without repentance.

Checkpoint
What does 'ghufran' (forgiveness) mean according to Al-Amir?
A) Erasing sin without condition
B) Covering sin through repentance or divine pardon
C) Punishing the sin anyway
D) Ignoring the sin

Key Takeaway
Forgiveness is defined as covering sin, and scholars agree that avoiding major sins expiates minor sins.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly repent from your sins, or do you rely on the hope of divine pardon to avoid the discomfort of tawbah? What is one specific sin you have been avoiding repenting from, and what will you do today to turn back to Allah?
?
This lesson says that avoiding major sins expiates minor sins—but are you using this as an excuse to commit minor sins freely, without concern? In your daily life, how often do you commit minor sins knowingly, assuming they are automatically forgiven? Will you continue with that assumption, or will you strive to avoid even minor sins as acts of devotion?
?
If forgiveness means covering the sin and erasing its trace, ask yourself: do you truly want your sins to be erased, or do you secretly cling to them? What does your heart desire more—your sin or Allah's forgiveness? Make a commitment to choose Allah's forgiveness over any sin, starting now.

The Certainty of Forgiveness: Probable, Not Certain
Al-Amir presents the kalam view and reasoning against certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the imams of kalam held that forgiveness of minor sins through avoidance is probable rather than certain.

Core Idea
Certainty of forgiveness would undermine Sharia by making sin permissible.

Having established that expiation occurs, we now explore the deeper question: is this forgiveness certain or merely probable? This lesson presents the reasoning of the kalam theologians, who argue that certainty would break the bonds of Sharia, and explains the conditional interpretation of the Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the opinion of the imams
Read the passage where Al-Amir states that the imams of kalam hold expiation is not certain but probable.
2
Understand the reasoning
Why would certainty be problematic? Because it would make sin like a permissible act with no consequence, weakening Sharia.
Think about the purpose of warning against sin.
3
Examine the verse interpretation
Notice how they interpret 'We will expiate' as 'if We will', analogously to another verse about forgiveness.
This shows divine will is involved.

This passage discusses the disagreement regarding the nature of forgiveness of minor sins through avoidance of major sins. The Two Opinions on Expiation of Minor Sins
Sanusi presents two opinions on expiation of minor sins by avoiding major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main opinions in kalam regarding whether avoiding major sins definitively expiates minor sins, and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Avoiding major sins brings forgiveness, but whether it is certain or conditional is debated.

This passage from Sanusi's Jawharat al-Tawhid addresses a delicate question: when a believer avoids major sins, are their minor sins automatically erased? The author presents two scholarly opinions, one from the theologians and another from jurists and Mu'tazila, and then weighs in. Let's explore the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'كفَّارَاتُ الذُّنوبِ...' to '...والأول هو الحقُّ' once without translating. Then read the translation. Notice the author's structure: definition, then two opinions.
The author uses a very concise style common in kalam texts.
2
Identify the two opinions and their bases
The first opinion (from a'immat al-kalam) says expiation is not certain but hoped for. The second (from jurists, hadith scholars, Mu'tazila) says it is definite. Note how each group interprets the same Qur'anic verse differently.
The difference hinges on whether forgiveness is unconditional or subject to God's will.
3
Understand the author's preferred opinion
The author states that the first opinion (theologians) is the truth. Reflect on why they argue that certainty would undermine the Sharia's seriousness.
This connects to the broader theme of balancing hope and fear in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفَّارَاتُ الذُّنوبِ وأَسبَابُ المَغفِرة كشتم بما (۱) لا يوجِبُ حَدًّا (۲) ، إذا اجتنب السَّرقة والزنا. وغَفْرُ الذَّنب : سَتْرُه بالتّوبة منه ، أو بالعفو ومحو أثره وأمن عاقبته(۳). يعني أنَّ هذا الحكم اختُلِفَ في قطعيته (٤) وظنيته، مع الاتفاق على تَرتُبِ التكفير (٥) على الاجتناب ، فذهب أئمة الكلام إلى أنَّه لا يجب التكفير على القطع ؛ بل يجوزُ ، ويغلب على الظَّن ويقوى فيها الرَّجاء ؛ لأنَّا لو قطعنا المُجتنب الكبائر بتكفير (٦) صغائره بالاجتناب ؛ لكانت له في حكم المُباح الَّذي يُقطع بأنَّه لا تِباعَةَ (۷) فيه ، وذلك نقض لعرى الشريعة (۸) ، فقوله تعالى: ﴿إن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ نُكَفِّرْ عَنكُم سَيِّئَاتِكُمْ﴾ [النساء: ٣١] ، معناه : إِنْ شِئنا (۹) ، حملا له (١٠) على قوله: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ، وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾ [النساء: ٤٨] ، هذا هو الحقُّ. وذهبَ جماعةٌ مِن الفقهاء والمُحدِّثينَ والمعتزلة: إلى أنَّ المكلَّف إذا اجتنب الكبائر كُفّرتْ صغائِرُه ،قطعا ، ولم يجز تعذيبه عليها، بمعنى أنَّه لا (1) أن يقع ؛ لقيام الأدلَّة السَّمعيَّة على عدم وقوعه ، كقوله تعالى: ﴿إن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَابِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ﴾ الآيةَ ، والنَّظم ظاهرُ في هذا الثَّاني، وهو أشهرُ مِن الأوَّل عندهم، ومبنى القولين جواز العقاب على الصغيرة(۲) وامتناعه ، والأول هو الحقُّ (۳) ، ثُمَّ المغفرة مُقيَّدة بمن أتى بالفرائض ؛ لحديث: ما مِن عبدٍ يُؤدِّي الصلوات الخمس، ويصومُ رمضانَ ويجتنب الكبائر السَّبع .. إلَّا فُتحت له ثمانية أبواب الجنة يوم القيامة؛ حتى أنها لتُصفّق (٥) (٦) الحديثَ ، وفي لفظ : «الصَّلوات الخمس ، والجمعة إلى الجمعة ، ورمضان إلى رمضان، مُكفِّرات لما بينهنَّ إذا اجتنبت الكبائر» (۷) ، هذا هو (٤).
Expiation of sins and causes of forgiveness: cursing with what does not necessitate a prescribed legal penalty, if one avoids theft and adultery. And forgiveness of the sin: covering it by repentance from it, or by pardon and erasing its trace and safety from its consequence. Meaning: this ruling was differed upon regarding its definitiveness and probability, with agreement on the sequence of expiation upon avoidance. So the leading scholars of kalam (aʼimmat al-kalām) held that expiation is not obligatory definitively, but is permissible, and it predominates in opinion and hope is strengthened in it; because if we were certain for the one who avoids major sins that his minor sins are expiated by avoidance, then for him it would be in the ruling of the permissible which one is certain that there is no consequence in it, and that would be a breaking of the bonds of the Sharia. So His saying, the Exalted: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will expiate from you your misdeeds' [al-Nisa 31] – its meaning is 'if We will', interpreting it in light of His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' [al-Nisa 48]. This is the truth. And a group of jurists, hadith scholars, and the Muʿtazila held that when the morally responsible person avoids major sins, his minor sins are expiated definitely, and it is not permissible to punish him for them, meaning that it does not occur, due to the scriptural evidences indicating its non-occurrence, like His saying, the Exalted: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden' – the verse, and the wording is apparent in this second [view], and it is more famous than the first among them. And the basis of the two opinions is the permissibility of punishment for minor sins and its impossibility, and the first is the truth. Then forgiveness is conditioned upon performing the obligatory acts, due to the hadith: 'There is no servant who performs the five prayers, fasts Ramadan, and avoids the seven major sins... except that eight gates of Paradise will be opened for him on the Day of Resurrection, such that they will be clapping' – the hadith, and in another wording: 'The five prayers, Jumuʿah to Jumuʿah, and Ramadan to Ramadan are expiations for what is between them, when major sins are avoided.' This is it.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on the ruling of expiation of minor sins by avoiding major sins. It begins by defining expiation (kaffārāt) and forgiveness (ghufr). The author explains that forgiveness is either covering the sin through repentance or through divine pardon and removal of its consequence. Then he states that the ruling—whether avoiding major sins definitely expiates minor sins—is disputed in terms of its certainty versus probability, although there is consensus that expiation is contingent upon avoidance.

The first opinion, held by the leading theologians (aʼimmat al-kalām), is that expiation is not definitively obligatory but is permissible and strongly hoped for. They argue that if avoiding major sins were certain to absolve one of all minor sins, then the person would be in the position of one who is certain he faces no consequences, which would undermine the fabric of Islamic law. They interpret the verse 'If you avoid the major sins... We will expiate from you your misdeeds' (al-Nisa 31) as conditional upon God's will, similar to the verse 'But He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (al-Nisa 48). Thus, it is a promise subject to divine will, not a guarantee.

The second opinion, held by some jurists, hadith scholars, and the Muʿtazila, is that avoidance of major sins definitely expiates minor sins, so much so that punishment for them is impossible. They appeal to the apparent meaning of the same verse and consider this view more famous. The author then clarifies that the underlying issue is whether punishment for minor sins is permissible in the first place. He declares the first opinion (the theologians' view) to be the truth.

Finally, the author qualifies the discussion: forgiveness is tied to performing obligatory duties. He cites a hadith stating that performing the five prayers, fasting Ramadan, and avoiding the seven major sins result in the opening of eight gates of Paradise. Another version says that prayers, Jumuʿah, and Ramadan expiate between them when major sins are avoided. This shows that even with avoidance of major sins, one must still fulfill obligations for full forgiveness.

مغفرة
تكفير
قطع
ظن
جواز العقاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the verse 'If you avoid major sins... We will expiate from you your misdeeds' guarantees forgiveness without any condition or divine will.
Correction: The theologians interpret it as conditional upon God's will, meaning it is not a automatic guarantee but a promise subject to His wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of kalam scholars ('a'immat al-kalam), what is the ruling on expiation of minor sins when one avoids major sins?
A) It is definitively obligatory and certain.
B) It is permissible and strongly hoped for, but not certain.
C) It does not happen at all.
D) It only happens if one also performs supererogatory acts.

Key Takeaway
The theologians hold that expiation is not certain but hoped for, while others hold it is definite; the author sides with the theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly believe that by avoiding the 'big' sins, your 'small' sins are automatically forgiven—so you don't really need to repent from them, watch your tongue, or examine your heart? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If forgiveness for minor sins is not guaranteed by merely avoiding major sins, but depends on God's will, what does that mean for the state of your heart right now? Are you presumptuous or genuinely hopeful?
?
What would it look like today to live with the realization that your minor sins matter—that they are not automatically wiped out—without falling into despair? How will you change one specific habit in light of this?

Forgiveness Conditioned on Obligatory Acts
Sanusi concludes with the condition of obligatory acts for forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that forgiveness of sins is conditional upon performing obligatory duties, as evidenced by hadith.

Core Idea
Forgiveness requires both avoiding major sins and performing obligations.

After presenting the debate on expiation, the author adds a crucial qualification: forgiveness is tied to performing the obligatory acts of worship. He supports this with a hadith that links prayers, fasting, and avoiding major sins to entrance into Paradise. This lesson unpacks that condition.

Guided Path
1

The author quotes multiple hadith wordings.
2
Identify the condition for forgiveness
The passage states that forgiveness is conditioned upon performing the obligatory acts (الفرائض). Note the specific acts mentioned in the hadith: five daily prayers, fasting Ramadan, and avoiding the seven major sins.
Avoiding major sins is already covered, but here it is linked to the obligations.
3
Reflect on the connection between actions and forgiveness
Think about why the author adds this condition. How does it balance the earlier discussion? It emphasizes that while God is forgiving, He also demands worship and adherence to commands.
This addresses the broader theme of human responsibility.

Expiation of Minor Sins: Two Views
Al-Sanusi presents two views on expiation of minor sins through avoiding major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly views on whether avoiding major sins automatically expiates minor sins, and the condition of performing obligatory acts for forgiveness.

Core Idea
Avoiding major sins alone does not automatically expiate minor sins without performing obligations.

This passage presents a key debate in Islamic theology: does avoiding major sins guarantee forgiveness of minor sins? Al-Sanusi presents two contrasting views and clarifies the correct position according to Sunni orthodoxy. He also adds an important condition from hadith that forgiveness is tied to fulfilling obligatory acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage along with its English translation. Notice the structure: a group's view, the scriptural evidence, the basis of the two views, the author's choice, and the condition linked to obligations.
The passage uses technical terms like 'takfir', 'saghair', 'kabair'.
2
Identify the two views
Identify the view that avoiding major sins definitively expiates minor sins (the group's view) and the opposing view that punishment is possible. The author declares the first view as the truth.
The group includes jurists, hadith scholars, and Mu'tazilites. Their view is that expiation is certain.
3
Understand the condition
Note that forgiveness is restricted to those who perform obligatory acts. The hadith about the five prayers and fasting Ramadan supports this. This shows that avoiding major sins alone is not sufficient.
The hadith mentions avoiding seven major sins, but other versions mention avoiding major sins generally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبَ جماعةٌ مِن الفقهاء والمُحدِّثينَ والمعتزلة: إلى أنَّ المكلَّف إذا اجتنب الكبائر كُفّرتْ صغائِرُه قطعاً، ولم يجز تعذيبه عليها، بمعنى أنَّه لا أن يقع؛ لقيام الأدلَّة السَّمعيَّة على عدم وقوعه، كقوله تعالى: ﴿إِن تَجْتَنِبُوا كَبَائِرَ مَا تُنْهَوْنَ عَنْهُ﴾ الآية، والنَّظم ظاهرٌ في هذا الثَّاني، وهو أشهرُ مِن الأوَّل عندهم، ومبنى القولين جواز العقاب على الصغيرة وامتناعه، والأول هو الحقُّ، ثُمَّ المغفرة مُقيَّدة بمن أتى بالفرائض؛ لحديث: ما مِن عبدٍ يُؤدِّي الصلوات الخمس، ويصوم رمضان ويجتنب الكبائر السَّبع .. إلَّا فُتحت له ثمانية أبواب الجنة يوم القيامة؛ حتى أنها لتُصفّق الحديثَ، وفي لفظ: «الصَّلوات الخمس، والجمعة إلى الجمعة، ورمضان إلى رمضان، مُكفِّرات لما بينهنَّ إذا اجتنبت الكبائر»، هذا هو
And a group of jurists, hadith scholars, and Mu'tazilites held that the responsible person, if he avoids major sins, his minor sins are expiated definitively, and it is not permissible to punish him for them, meaning that it does not occur; due to the evidence from revelation establishing its non-occurrence, like His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden' [Quran 4:31], and the wording is apparent in this second [view], and it is more famous than the first among them, and the basis of the two views is the permissibility of punishment for the minor sin and its impossibility, and the first is the truth, then forgiveness is restricted to whoever performs the obligations; due to the hadith: 'There is no servant who performs the five prayers, fasts Ramadan, and avoids the seven major sins ... except that eight gates of Paradise are opened for him on the Day of Resurrection; even that they clap' the hadith, and in another wording: 'The five prayers, Friday to Friday, and Ramadan to Ramadan are expiations for what is between them if major sins are avoided,' this is it.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal dispute regarding the expiation (takfīr) of minor sins (saghā'ir) when one avoids major sins (kabā'ir). The author first states the view of a group of scholars, including jurists, hadith scholars, and Mu'tazilites: they hold that if a morally responsible person avoids major sins, his minor sins are definitively expiated, and it is not permissible (lā yajūz) for him to be punished for them. The phrase 'meaning that it does not occur' explains that this is not a rational impossibility but a revealed certainty based on scriptural evidence, such as the verse from Surah al-Nisa' (4:31). The author notes that the wording of the verse is apparent in supporting the second view (that minor sins are expiated), and this view is more famous among them. The two views are built on the question of whether punishment for minor sins is permissible (jawāz al-ʿiqāb) or not. The author then declares the first view—that punishment is possible and expiation is not necessary—to be the truth (al-ḥaqq). He then adds a key condition: forgiveness is restricted to one who performs the obligatory acts (al-farā'iḍ). He supports this with a hadith about the eight gates of Paradise being opened for those who perform the five prayers, fast Ramadan, and avoid the seven major sins. Another version of the hadith states that the five prayers, Friday to Friday, and Ramadan to Ramadan are expiations for what is between them if major sins are avoided. The passage thus clarifies that while avoiding major sins is a condition for expiation of minor sins, it is not automatic; it also requires fulfilling obligations.

صغائر
كبائر
الفرائض
جواز العقاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that avoiding major sins automatically expiates all minor sins without any condition.
Correction: The passage shows that forgiveness is contingent on performing obligatory acts, as indicated by the hadith.

Checkpoint

C) Minor sins are never punished if major sins are avoided
D) Only repentance expiates minor sins

Key Takeaway
Avoiding major sins is necessary but not sufficient for expiation of minor sins; performing obligatory acts is also required based on hadith.

Expiation of Sins: Minor vs. Major
Al-Sanusi lists acts that expiate minor sins and clarifies the condition regarding major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between how minor and major sins are expiated, and the role of good deeds in expiating minor sins.

Core Idea
Minor sins are cleansed by good deeds; major sins require repentance or Allah's grace.

Al-Sanusi explains that while major sins require repentance or divine grace, minor sins can be expiated through various acts of worship such as wudu, prayer, fasting, and Hajj. This passage clarifies the true meaning of the condition that these acts are contingent upon avoiding major sins.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the discussion on expiation of sins.
Notice the two categories of sins: minor and major.
2
Identify the evidence
Note the Quranic verse and hadith cited to support that good deeds erase minor sins.
The verse is from Surah Hud (11:114) and the hadith is 'Follow the evil deed with a good deed...'
3
Understand the condition
Al-Sanusi clarifies that the condition of avoiding major sins does not negate the expiation of minor sins by good deeds.
The condition means major sins are not expiated by these acts, not that the acts become invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كفَّارَاتُ الذُّنوبِ وأَسبَابُ المَغفِرة. الصَّحيح، وأمَّا الكبائر فلا يُكفّرها إِلَّا التَّوبةُ أو فضل الله تعالى. وأشار بقوله: (وَجَا الْوُضُو يُكَفِّرُ) الصَّغائر أيضا .. إلى عدم انحصار تكفيرها في اجتناب الكبائر لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْحَسَنَتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ﴾ [هود: ١١٤]، وفي الحديث: «وَأَتَّبع السيئة الحسنة تمحها»، وأراد بقوله: (وَجَاء) أي: في السُّنَّة؛ إذ فيها: مَن تَوضّأ نحو وضوئي هذا، ثُمَّ قامَ فركع ركعتين لا يُحدِّث فيهما نَفْسَهُ) يعني بسوء «غُفِرَ له ما تقدَّم مِن ذنبه» وفي رواية: «لا يتوضأ رجلٌ مُسلِم فيُحسن الوضوء فيُصلِّي صلاة .. إِلَّا غُفر له ما بينه وبين الصَّلاة التي تليها» وكذا الصَّلوات الخمس، وكذا رمضان، وكذا الحج المبرور، والكلُّ مَشروط باجتناب الكبائر كما في الصحيحين، على أنَّه إن كان هناك كبائر لا يُكفّرها إلَّا التَّوبَةُ أو فَضْلُ الله لا الوضوء أو غيره، ومعنى هذا بيان لمعنى الشرطية، لا كما يوهمه ظاهر اللفظ من أنه إذا لم يجتنب الكبائر لا يكفر هذه المذكورات شيئاً، وليس كذلك، بل المراد إن لم يحصل الاجتناب المذكور فلا بد للكبائر من توبة، لأنها لا تكفر بهذه المذكورات، فلا ينافي أن هذه المذكورات تكفر الصغائر مطلقاً.
Expiation of sins and causes of forgiveness. The correct [view] is that major sins are not expiated except by repentance or the grace of Allah Almighty. And he indicated by his saying: 'And wudu has come expiating' minor sins as well... to the non-exclusivity of their expiation in avoiding major sins, due to His statement: 'Indeed, good deeds remove evil deeds' [Quran 11:114], and in the hadith: 'Follow the evil deed with a good deed, it erases it.' And he intended by his saying: 'And it came' i.e., in the Sunnah; for in it: 'Whoever performs wudu like this wudu of mine, then stands and prays two rak'ahs not speaking to himself' meaning with evil 'his previous sins are forgiven.' And in another narration: 'No Muslim man performs wudu and does it well, then prays a prayer... except that he is forgiven for what is between it and the next prayer.' Likewise the five prayers, likewise Ramadan, likewise the accepted Hajj, and all are conditioned on avoiding major sins as in the two Sahihs, meaning that if there are major sins, nothing expiates them except repentance or the grace of Allah, not wudu or other. And the meaning of this is an explanation of the meaning of the conditionality, not as the apparent wording suggests that if he does not avoid major sins, none of these mentioned things expiate anything. It is not so; rather, the intended meaning is that if the mentioned avoidance does not occur, then major sins require repentance because they are not expiated by these mentioned things, so it does not contradict that these mentioned things expiate minor sins absolutely.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage defines the means of expiation (kafārāt) for sins and the causes of forgiveness. The author begins by affirming the correct view: major sins (kabā'ir) are not expiated except by repentance (tawba) or the sheer grace (faḍl) of Allah. He then interprets the line from the poem 'And wudu has come expiating minor sins' to indicate that expiation of minor sins is not exclusively dependent on avoiding major sins; rather, there are other means. He cites the Quranic verse 'Indeed, good deeds remove evil deeds' (11:114) and the hadith 'Follow the evil deed with a good deed, it erases it.' He explains that the phrase 'And it came' refers to the Sunnah, giving examples: the hadith that performing wudu and praying two rak'ahs without evil thoughts forgives previous sins, and another prayer forgives between it and the next. He then lists other acts: the five daily prayers, fasting Ramadan, and the accepted Hajj; all are expiatory for minor sins. However, he notes that all these are conditioned on avoiding major sins, as found in the two Sahih collections. The author clarifies the meaning of this condition: it does not mean that if one does not avoid major sins, these acts expiate nothing. Rather, it means that if one does not avoid major sins, those major sins require repentance because they are not expiated by these acts. But this does not prevent these acts from expiating minor sins in any case. The passage thus distinguishes between the expiation of minor sins (which can occur through various good deeds) and the expiation of major sins (which requires repentance or divine grace).

Key Terms
كفارات
الذنوب
أسباب المغفرة
التوبة
فضل الله
الوضوء
الحج المبرور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if one commits major sins, good deeds like wudu and prayer are entirely ineffective.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such acts still expiate minor sins; major sins require separate repentance or Allah's grace.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which sins cannot be expiated by wudu, prayers, or Hajj?
A) All sins
B) Minor sins only
C) Major sins (kabā'ir)
D) Only intentional sins

Key Takeaway
Minor sins are expiated by good deeds, while major sins require repentance or Allah's grace; the condition of avoiding major sins does not nullify the expiation of minor sins.

?

Good Deeds Are Not Voided by Major Sins
Commentary clarifies that major sins don't nullify good deeds' expiation of minor sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the condition of avoiding major sins does not render good deeds useless; rather, good deeds still expiate minor sins and can alleviate major sins.

Core Idea
Good deeds still count even with major sins — they expiate minor sins and may lighten major ones.

This passage addresses a common concern: if a person commits major sins, are their good deeds (like wudu or prayer) worthless? The commentator clarifies, citing Imam al-Nawawi, that the condition of avoiding major sins only affects full expiation of minor sins; but good deeds still have value, including reducing the burden of major sins and earning rewards.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between 'expiation' (تكفير) and 'alleviation' (تخفيف).
The passage is a clarification, so pay attention to what it refutes.
2
Understand the condition and its limits
The condition 'avoiding major sins' is not a precondition for any benefit. It only means that minor sins are fully expiated when major sins are absent. Al-Nawawi clarifies that good deeds still expiate something even with major sins.
3
Explore the three outcomes
The passage describes three scenarios: (1) minor sins only → full expiation, (2) major sins present → alleviation hoped, (3) no sins → reward written. Notice the different terms used for each.
The variant 'يرجى' (it is hoped) reflects the possibility of divine mercy.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself why this clarification is important. It prevents despair and shows that good deeds always have value, regardless of one's state. This underscores Allah's mercy and the importance of continuous good actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أنَّه إن كان هناك كبائر لا يُكفّرها إلَّا التّوبَةُ أو فَضْلُ الله لا الوضوء أو الصَّلاة، وليس المراد أنَّه مع الكبائر لا يُكفّر شيئًا، كما حرَّره النووي رحمه الله تعالى، ثُمَّ المراد أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِن هذه الأمور صالح للتكفير، له يخفف عنه منها، وإن صادف كبيرة أو كبائر رُجِيَ [نسخة: يُرجى] أن يخفف عنه منها، وإن لم يُصادف صغيرة ولا كبيرةً كُتبَ له به حسنة ورُفعت به درجاتٌ.
On the basis that if there are major sins, they are not expiated except by repentance or the grace of Allah, not by wudu or prayer. It is not meant that with major sins, [these acts] do not expiate anything, as al-Nawawi, may Allah have mercy on him, clarified. Then the meaning is that each one of these matters is suitable for expiation: it alleviates from him from them, and if it encounters a major sin or major sins, it is hoped [variant: perhaps] that it alleviates from him from them, and if it does not encounter a minor sin nor a major sin, a good deed is written for him and degrees are raised.
Source pages p. 528

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of the condition 'avoiding major sins'. The author explains that this condition does not mean that if major sins exist, the good deeds (wudu, prayer, etc.) are entirely ineffective. Al-Nawawi is cited to support that these acts still expiate minor sins absolutely. For major sins, they do not expiate, but they may alleviate punishment or raise the hope of reduction. If a person has no sin (minor or major), the act is still rewarded and raises his rank. The condition is understood as: if major sins are absent, these acts fully expiate minor sins; if major sins are present, they are not expiated by these acts but by repentance, while the acts still benefit in other ways.

Correction: The passage clarifies that good deeds still expiate minor sins and may alleviate major sins, and even if no sin exists, they are rewarded.

Checkpoint

A) They become invalid
B) They expiate the major sins as well
C) They still expiate minor sins and may alleviate major sins
D) No reward is written

Key Takeaway
Good deeds remain beneficial regardless of major sins; they expiate minor sins, alleviate major sins, and increase reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Sins as Diseases, Good Deeds as Medicines
Analogy of sins and diseases, condition of acceptance, and expiation scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy of sins as diseases and good deeds as medicines, the condition of acceptance for expiation, and the distinction between sins against Allah and sins against people in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Good deeds are medicines that expiate sins, but only if accepted, and only for sins against Allah.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary introduces the category of 'Sam'iyyat' (matters from revelation) and uses a powerful analogy: sins are like diseases, good deeds like specific medicines. However, expiation is not automatic; the deeds must be accepted, and the reward remains intact—contra the Mu'tazila view. Importantly, expiation only applies to sins against Allah; sins against people require a different mechanism: mutual offsetting of good and bad deeds on the Day of Judgment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Analogy
Read the passage carefully and note the comparison between sins/diseases and good deeds/medicine. Recognize that each sin requires a specific expiation known only to Allah.
2
Identify the Two Positions
Distinguish the Sunni view (expiation with reward remaining) from the Mu'tazila view (reward dropped in exchange for expiation). Understand which is the correct doctrine according to the author.
The passage states the apparent meaning of hadiths supports the Sunni position.
3
Learn the Crucial Distinction
Note that expiation covers only sins against Allah; sins against people (e.g., oppression) are not expiated by worship but require compensation via mutual offsetting of good and bad deeds between the parties on Judgment Day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات وأحسنُ مِن هذا أنَّ الذُّنوب كالأمراض ، والأعمال الصالحة كالأدوية، فكما لكل نوع من أنواع الأمراض نوع من أنواع الأدوية لا ينجعُ فيه غيره .. كذلك المُكفراتُ مع الذُّنوبِ ، وتوزيع ذلكَ مَوكُولٌ إلى علم الله تعالى، وظواهر الأحاديث أنَّ هذه العبادات لا تُكفّر إلَّا إذا كانتْ مقبولةٌ ، والمراد : أنَّها مُكفّرة للصَّغائر مع بقاء ثوابها كما هو مذهب أهل الحقِّ ، لا أنَّها يُسْقَط ثوابها في نظيرها كما ذهب إليه المعتزلة. ثُمَّ التكفيرُ إنَّما هو للذُّنوب المُتعلقة بحقوق الله تعالى لا المتعلقة بحقوق الآدميين؛ لأنَّها إنَّما يقع النظر فيها بالمُقَاصَّة مع الحسنات والسيئات.
Section on Sam'iyyat (matters from revelation). And better than this is that sins are like diseases, and good deeds are like medicines. For just as for each type of disease there is a type of medicine that works for it and not another—similarly the expiations with [for] sins. And the distribution of that is entrusted to the knowledge of Allah, exalted. And the apparent meanings of the hadiths [indicate] that these acts of worship do not expiate unless they are accepted. And the intended meaning: that they are expiating for minor sins while their reward remains—as is the doctrine of the people of truth—not that their reward is dropped in exchange for them, as the Mu'tazila held. Then expiation is only for sins related to the rights of Allah, not those related to the rights of human beings; because consideration in them only occurs by mutual offsetting with good deeds and bad deeds.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage begins with the section heading 'Sam'iyyat' (matters derived from revelation). It then presents an analogy: sins are like diseases and good deeds like specific medicines; just as each disease requires a specific cure, so each type of sin may require a specific expiatory act. The exact distribution of which expiation covers which sin is known only to Allah. Then the author addresses the condition of acceptance (qubool): the hadiths indicate these acts must be accepted to expiate. The correct Sunni position is that they expiate minor sins while still retaining the reward for the act—contra the Mu'tazila who held that the reward is canceled in exchange for expiation. Finally, expiation applies only to sins against Allah, not to sins against other humans (like oppression). Sins against people require compensation, such as giving of one's good deeds to the victim or taking their bad deeds.

Key Terms
السمعيات
المُكفرات
مذهب أهل الحق
المعتزلة
حقوق الآدميين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that expiation removes the reward of the good deed, or that sins against people can be expiated by worship alone.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the reward remains (Sunni view) and that sins against people require mutual offsetting of deeds, not mere worship.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes sins against Allah from sins against people?
A) Sins against people are never forgiven
B) Sins against people require mutual offsetting of deeds
C) Both types are expiated the same way
D) Only sins against Allah can be expiated by good deeds

Key Takeaway
Expiation applies only to sins against Allah, requires accepted good deeds, and preserves the reward; sins against people are resolved through mutual offsetting of deeds on Judgment Day.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson insists acceptance is a condition—how often do you pray for your deeds to be accepted, and what would change if you truly believed acceptance is not guaranteed?
?
Think of a sin you committed against another person that you have never directly addressed with them. Have you ever tried to 'make up for it' through extra prayers or charity, hoping it would erase that sin? This lesson teaches that sins against people are not expiated by worship—so what concrete step will you take this week to seek forgiveness from that person?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that by expiating a sin, the reward of the good deed is lost. Is there a part of you that feels you 'deserve' reward for your obedience—that Allah owes you? How does the Ahl al-Haqq view (reward preserved) challenge your assumptions about God's generosity and your own sense of entitlement?

The Scope of Expiatory Acts: Major Sins Excluded
Clarification that good deeds do not expiate major sins

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that good deeds like prayer expiate only minor sins, not major ones, and you will learn the three possible outcomes when performing such deeds.

Core Idea
Good deeds expiate only minor sins; major sins require repentance.

This passage from the commentary on the section of samiyyat clarifies a common misunderstanding: while acts of worship can expiate minor sins, they do not erase major sins. Imam Nawawi's clarification sets the stage, and then the author outlines three scenarios for the effect of these deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the initial clarification about major sins.
Notice the mention of Imam Nawawi.
2
Identify the three outcomes
List the three possible outcomes when a good deed encounters a sin or no sin.
These are explicit in the text.
3
Understand the limitation
Reflect on why major sins are excluded and what this implies for the role of repentance.
The passage does not elaborate on repentance, but it is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات. الصَّلاة ، وليس المراد أنَّه مع الكبائر لا يُكفّر شي (١) ، كما حرَّره النووي رحمه الله تعالى (٢) ، ثُمَّ المراد أنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ مِن هذه الأمور صالح للتكفير، يخفَّف له (٣) أن فإن وجدَ ما يُكفِّره مِن الصَّغائر كفّره ، وإن صادف كبيرة أو كبائر رُجِي) عنه منها ، وإن لم يُصادف صغيرة ولا كبيرةً كُتبَ له به حَسنات ورُفِعَتْ به درجاتٌ وأحسنُ مِن هذا أنَّ الذَّنوب كالأمراض (٤) ، والأعمال الصالحة كالأدوية، فكما لكل نوع من أنواع الأمراض نوع من أنواع الأدوية لا ينجعُ فيه غيره .. كذلك المُكفراتُ مع الذَّنوبِ ، وتوزيع ذلكَ مَوكُولٌ إلى علم الله تعالى، وظواهر الأحاديث أنَّ هذه العبادات لا تُكفّر إلَّا إذا كانتْ مقبولةٌ ، والمراد : أنَّها مُكفّرة للصَّغائر مع بقاء ثوابها كما هو مذهب أهل الحقِّ ، لا أنَّها يُسْقَط ثوابها في نظيرها كما ذهب إليه المعتزلة. ثُمَّ التكفيرُ إنَّما هو للذُّنوب المُتعلقة بحقوق الله تعالى لا المتعلقة بحقوق الآدميين؛ لأنَّها إنَّما يقع النظر فيها بالمُقَاصَّة (٥) والسيئات مع الحسنات.
The section on audible matters. The prayer, and the intended meaning is not that it does not expel anything with major sins, as Nawawi, may God have mercy on him, clarified. Then the intended meaning is that each one of these matters is suitable for expiation. It is alleviated for him... For if it finds what expiates it from minor sins, it expiates it; and if it encounters a major sin or major sins, it is hoped that [it alleviates] it from them; and if it encounters neither a minor nor a major sin, good deeds are written for him and degrees are raised for him. Better than this is that sins are like diseases, and righteous deeds are like medicines; for just as each type of disease has a type of medicine that is not effective for others, similarly the expiating deeds [relate] to sins. The distribution of that is entrusted to the knowledge of God Almighty. The apparent meanings of the hadiths are that these acts of worship do not expiate unless they are accepted. The intended meaning is that they expiate minor sins while preserving their reward, as is the doctrine of the people of truth, not that their reward is canceled in exchange as the Mu'tazila held. Then expiation is only for sins related to the rights of God, not those related to the rights of human beings, because they are considered only through mutual settlement, and evil deeds along with good deeds.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the section on samiyyat (audible matters) explains the doctrine of expiation (takfir) through good deeds such as prayer, wudu, etc. It clarifies that these acts do not expiate major sins. The author first quotes Imam Nawawi's clarification that the exception applies to major sins, meaning they require repentance. Then it is stated that each of these acts is capable of expiating minor sins: if it encounters a minor sin, it expiates it; if it meets a major sin, it is hoped it alleviates it; if it meets neither, it is recorded as a good deed raising one's rank. The author then offers an analogy: sins are like diseases, and good deeds are like specific medicines, each suited to its type—distributing them is known only to God. The apparent meaning of hadiths is that these acts expiate only if they are accepted by God, and they expiate minor sins while preserving their reward. This is the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth), as opposed to the Mu'tazila who held that reward could be canceled in exchange. Finally, expiation applies only to sins concerning the rights of God; sins concerning the rights of people are dealt with on the Day of Judgment through mutual settlement (muqassa).

Key Terms
صلاة
كبرائر
صغائر
تكفير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that performing prayers or other good deeds automatically erases all sins, including major ones.
Correction: This passage clarifies that major sins are not expiated by ordinary good deeds; they require specific repentance (tawbah). The expiation of good deeds only applies to minor sins.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a good deed encounters a major sin?
A) It expiates it fully.
B) It is hoped it alleviates it.
C) It has no effect.
D) It increases the sin.

Key Takeaway
Good deeds expiate minor sins, alleviate major sins (but do not erase them), and if no sin is present, they earn rewards.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a major sin you have committed and have not yet sincerely repented from. Are you relying on your daily prayers to erase it, knowing this lesson says prayer only alleviates major sins? What is one concrete step you will take today to repent?

The Analogy of Sins as Diseases
Analogy comparing sins to diseases and good deeds to medicines

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy that sins are like diseases and good deeds are like specific medicines, with the distribution of expiation known only to God.

Core Idea
Each sin has its appropriate expiation, like a specific medicine for a disease.

Having explained the basic rules, the author presents a deeper analogy to illustrate the relationship between sins and expiatory deeds. Sins are like various diseases, and good deeds are like medicines specifically suited to each type. This underscores the precision of divine justice and the mystery of how expiation works.

Guided Path
1

Identify the key comparison

The author first quotes Imam Nawawi's clarification that the exception applies to major sins, meaning they require repentance. Then it is stated that each of these acts is capable of expiating minor sins: if it encounters a minor sin, it expiates it; if it meets a major sin, it is hoped it alleviates it; if it meets neither, it is recorded as a good deed raising one's rank. The author then offers an analogy: sins are like diseases, and good deeds are like specific medicines, each suited to its type—distributing them is known only to God. The apparent meaning of hadiths is that these acts expiate only if they are accepted by God, and they expiate minor sins while preserving their reward. This is the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth), as opposed to the Mu'tazila who held that reward could be canceled in exchange. Finally, expiation applies only to sins concerning the rights of God; sins concerning the rights of people are dealt with on the Day of Judgment through mutual settlement (muqassa).

Key Terms
ذنوب
أمراض
أدوية
مكفرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any good deed can expiate any sin.
Correction: The analogy shows that expiation is specific: just as a medicine for one disease may not work for another, a specific good deed may be required for a particular sin.

Checkpoint
What does the analogy compare sins to?
A) Fire
B) Diseases
C) Thorns
D) Darkness

Key Takeaway
Sins are like diseases requiring specific treatments, and only God knows the precise correspondence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific sin you struggle with. Is there a particular good deed you have been avoiding that could be its 'medicine'? What is stopping you from taking that step today?
?
Do you often rely on general acts of worship (like extra prayers) hoping they will cover all your sins, ignoring the need for targeted repentance? How does this analogy challenge you to be more precise in your expiation?
?
The passage says only God knows the exact correspondence between sins and expiations. Does this humble you, or does it unsettle you? How will you respond to the fact that your efforts to atone might miss the mark without divine guidance?

Conditions of Expiation: Acceptance and Limits
Conditions for expiation: acceptance and scope limited to divine rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that expiation requires acceptance by God, that the reward remains intact unlike the Mu'tazila view, and that expiation only applies to sins against God, not against other people.

Core Idea
Expiation preserves reward and applies only to divine rights, not human rights.

The passage concludes with two crucial conditions: first, expiation only occurs if the good deed is accepted by God, and second, it only applies to sins against God, not those against fellow humans. The author contrasts the Sunni view (reward preserved) with the Mu'tazila view (reward canceled), and explains that sins against people require mutual settlement on Judgment Day.

Guided Path
1
Read the conditions
Read the Arabic and translation to identify the two conditions mentioned.
Condition 1: acceptance; Condition 2: scope.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila dispute
Compare the Sunni view (reward preserved) with the Mu'tazila view (reward canceled).
3
Grasp the limitation to divine rights
Reflect on why human rights are excluded and how they are resolved (muqassa).
This is a key distinction.

The section on audible matters. The prayer, and the intended meaning is not that it does not expel anything with major sins, as Nawawi, may God have mercy on him, clarified. Then the intended meaning is that each one of these matters is suitable for expiation. It is alleviated for him... For if it finds what expiates it from minor sins, it expiates it; and if it encounters a major sin or major sins, it is hoped that [it alleviates] it from them; and if it encounters neither a minor nor a major sin, good deeds are written for him and degrees are raised for him. Better than this is that sins are like diseases, and righteous deeds are like medicines; for just as each type of disease has a type of medicine that is not effective for others, similarly the expiating deeds [relate] to sins. The distribution of that is entrusted to the knowledge of God Almighty. The apparent meanings of the hadiths are that these acts of worship do not expiate unless they are accepted. The intended meaning is that they expiate minor sins while preserving their reward, as is the doctrine of the people of truth, not that their reward is canceled in exchange as the Mu'tazila held. Then expiation is only for sins related to the rights of God, not those related to the rights of human beings, because they are considered only through mutual settlement, and evil deeds along with good deeds.
Source pages p. 529

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the section on samiyyat (audible matters) explains the doctrine of expiation (takfir) through good deeds such as prayer, wudu, etc. It clarifies that these acts do not expiate major sins. The author first quotes Imam Nawawi's clarification that the exception applies to major sins, meaning they require repentance. Then it is stated that each of these acts is capable of expiating minor sins: if it encounters a minor sin, it expiates it; if it meets a major sin, it is hoped it alleviates it; if it meets neither, it is recorded as a good deed raising one's rank. This is the position of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth), as opposed to the Mu'tazila who held that reward could be canceled in exchange. Finally, expiation applies only to sins concerning the rights of God; sins concerning the rights of people are dealt with on the Day of Judgment through mutual settlement (muqassa).

The Meaning and Naming of the Last Day
Defines the Last Day and explains its naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the Last Day and the reasons it is called 'the last'.

Core Idea
The Last Day is the eternal period after the world with no night.

The commentator explains that the Last Day is the Day of Resurrection, beginning at the gathering and lasting eternally or until entrance into Paradise and Hell. It is named 'the last' for three distinct reasons. Let's read the passage and explore each reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that the verse line declares the truth of the Last Day and the horror of the standing, then the commentary begins explaining the Last Day.
Focus on the commentary part after 'فقال:'.
2
Identify the definition of the Last Day
The commentary gives two possible definitions: it is from the time of gathering until no end, or until people enter their final abodes. Note the flexibility in meaning.
3
Examine the three reasons for its name
The text lists three reasons: it is the last of limited times, there is no night after it, and it is the last day of the world. Understand each reason and how they reinforce the idea of finality.
These reasons are straightforward but emphasize the break from worldly cycles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٠٣. واليومُ الآخِرُ ثُمَّ هَوْلُ المُوقِفِ حَقٌّ فَخَفِّفْ يَا رَحِيمُ وَاسْعِفِ. ثُمَّ شرعَ في الكلام على زمن وقوع الحَشْرِ والحِساب وأهواله فقال: ( وَاليَوْمُ الآخِرُ) وهو يوم القيامة، والمراد به من وقت الحشرِ إلى ما لا يتناهى، أو إلى أنْ يَدخُلَ أهلُ الجَنَّةِ الجَنَّةَ وأهلُ النَّارِ النَّارَ، سُمِّي بذلك ؛ لأنَّه آخر الأوقات المحدودة (١) ، ولأنَّه لا ليلَ بعده ، ولأنَّه آخر أيَّام الدنيا. (ثُمَّ هَوْلُ المَوْقِفِ) أي: عظائمه وما يَنَالُ النَّاس فيه مِن الشَّدائد والمصائب ، كطُّول الوقوف ، والجامِ العَرَق (٢) النَّاسَ حتَّى يبلغ آذانهم ، ويذهب في الأرض سبعين ذراعاً، وتطاير الكتب بالأيمان والشمائل (٣)، ولزومها الأعناق، والمُسَاءَلَةِ ، وشهادة الألسنة والأيدي والأرجل والسمع والبصر والجلود
103. And the Last Day, then the terror of the standing is true, so lighten, O Merciful, and help. Then he began to speak about the time of the occurrence of the gathering and the reckoning and its terrors, and said: (And the Last Day) and it is the Day of Resurrection, and by it is meant from the time of the gathering to what has no end, or until the people of Paradise enter Paradise and the people of the Fire enter the Fire. It is named thus because it is the last of the limited times, and because there is no night after it, and because it is the last of the days of the world. (Then the terror of the standing) i.e., its great horrors and what befalls people therein of hardships and calamities, such as the length of standing, and the drowning of sweat overwhelming people until it reaches their ears, and it goes seventy cubits into the ground, and the flying of the books to the right and left, and their clinging to necks, and the questioning, and the testimony of tongues, hands, feet, hearing, sight, and skins.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is the commentary on verse 103 of the matn (poem). The verse states that the Last Day and the terror of the standing are true, and it implores the Merciful to lighten it and help. The commentary then explains the meaning of 'the Last Day' (al-yawm al-akhir): it is the Day of Resurrection, and it begins from the time of the gathering (hashr) and continues without end, or until the people of Paradise and Hell enter their abodes. It is named 'the last' because it is the final of the limited times, because there is no night after it, and because it is the last day of the worldly life. The next term, 'the terror of the standing' (hawl al-mawqif), refers to the great horrors and calamities that befall people: the long duration of standing, sweat covering people in such a way that it drowns them (iljam al-araq) until it reaches their ears and goes seventy cubits into the ground, the flying of records (books of deeds) to the right and left and their being made to cling to necks, the questioning, and the testimony of all body parts—tongues, hands, feet, hearing, sight, and skins—against the person.

Key Terms
اليوم الآخر
يوم القيامة
الحشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Last Day is a single 24-hour day like a worldly day.
Correction: It is a prolonged period from the gathering to eternity, with no night, so it is not equivalent to a worldly day.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which of the following is a reason the Last Day is called 'the last'?
A) Because it is the last of the limited times
B) Because it occurs after the Day of Judgment
C) Because it is the shortest day
D) Because it has a night and a day

Key Takeaway
The Last Day is the final, unending day that follows this world, named for its finality and lack of night.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Last Day is the end of all limited times, what have you filled your limited days with that will last into that unending day?
?
You will stand in the terror of the gathering (هَوْلُ المُوقِفِ) – are you living today as if that moment is imminent, or are you anesthetizing yourself with distractions?
?
There will be no night after the Last Day – no rest, no break, no escape. What unforgiven sin or unfulfilled duty are you assuming will be hidden in a darkness that will never come?

The Terrors of the Standing
Enumerates the horrors of the standing on the Day of Judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specific horrors that occur during the standing on the Day of Judgment as described in the commentary.

Core Idea
The standing brings overwhelming terrors: long waiting, drowning sweat, flying books, and body testimony.

After defining the Last Day, the commentator turns to the terror of the standing (mawqif). He enumerates several intense phenomena that will afflict people. This lesson unpacks each horror mentioned in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the terror of the standing

2

The list is vivid and meant to convey the gravity of the situation.
3
Reflect on the testimony of the limbs
Notice that the last item includes the testimony of tongues, hands, feet, hearing, sight, and skins. This emphasizes that every part of a person will bear witness against them.
This is a unique aspect of Islamic eschatology.

The Last Day: Definition and Naming
Definition and naming of the Last Day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the Last Day and the three reasons it is called by that name.

Core Idea
The Last Day is the final stage of existence with no night after it.

The commentator begins by defining the Last Day, explaining its scope and why it is given this name. This foundational understanding prepares us for the detailed descriptions of its events. Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator defines the Last Day.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage gives two possible meanings for the time span of the Last Day: from the gathering to eternity, or until Paradise and Hell are filled. Understand both.
These are not contradictory but different perspectives.
3
Analyze the three naming reasons
List the three reasons given for why this day is called the Last Day: last of limited times, no night after it, last of worldly days. Reflect on the significance.
These reasons emphasize its finality and uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ شرعَ في الكلام على زمن وقوع الحَشْرِ والحِساب وأهواله فقال: (وَاليَوْمُ الآخِرُ) وهو يوم القيامة، والمراد به من وقت الحشرِ إلى ما لا يتناهى، أو إلى أنْ يَدخُلَ أهلُ الجَنَّةِ الجَنَّةَ وأهلُ النَّارِ النَّارَ، سُمِّي بذلك؛ لأنَّه آخر الأوقات المحدودة، ولأنَّه لا ليلَ بعده، ولأنَّه آخر أيَّام الدنيا. (ثُمَّ هَوْلُ المَوْقِفِ) أي: عظائمه وما يَنَالُ النَّاس فيه مِن الشَّدائد والمصائب، كطُّول الوقوف، وإلجام العَرَق النَّاسَ حتَّى يبلغ آذانهم، ويذهب في الأرض سبعين ذراعاً، وتطاير الكتب بالأيمان والشمائل، ولزومها الأعناق، والمُسَاءَلَةِ، وشهادة الألسنة والأيدي والأرجل والسمع والبصر والجلود والأرض والليل والنهار والحَفَظة الكرام، وتغير الألوان، والظاهر كما قال السعد: أنَّه لا ينال شيءٌ ممَّا ذُكِرَ الأنبياء ولا الأولياء ولا سائر الصُلَحَاءِ؛ لقوله تعالى: تَتَنَزَّلُ عَلَيْهِمُ الْمَلَائِكَةُ [فصلت: 30] الآية لَا يَحْزُنُهُمُ الْفَزَعُ الْأَكْبَرُ [الأنبياء: 103]، وخوفُ الأنبياء والملائكة خَوْفُ إعظام وإجلال، وإن كانوا آمنين عذاب الله، وقوله: (حَقٌّ)، أي: ثابت لا محالة، خبر «اليوم الآخر» وما عُطف عليه؛ فيجب الإيمان به؛ لورُودِه كتاباً وسُنَّةً وإجماع المسلمين عليه، قال تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا النَّاسُ اتَّقُوا رَبَّكُمْ إِنَّ زَلْزَلَةَ السَّاعَةِ شَيْءٍ عَظِيمُ [الحج: 1] إلى قوله: وَلَكِنَّ عَذَابَ اللَّهِ شَدِيدٌ [الحج: 2]، وإِنَّا نَخَافُ مِن رَّبِّنَا يَوْمًا عَبُوسًا قَمْطَرِيرًا [الإنسان: 10] يَوْمًا يَجْعَلُ الْوِلْدَانَ شِيبًا [المزمل: 17] لِكُلِّ امْرِئٍ مِنْهُمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ شَأْنٌ يُغْنِيهِ [عبس: 37]، يَوْمَ تَبْيَضُ وُجُوهٌ وَتَسْوَدُّ وُجُوهُ [آل عمران: 106]. وأشار بقوله: (فَخَفِّفْ يَا رَحِيمُ) أهواله وعظائِمَه (وَاسْعِفِ)، أي: وأعنا عليه.. إلى أنَّه مُختَلِفٌ باختلاف أحوال النَّاس، فيُشدَّدُ على الكُفَّار حتّى يجدوا من طوله الغاية، ويُتوسّط على فسقة المؤمنين، ويُخَفَّفُ على الصَّالحين حتّى يكون كصلاة ركعتين، وكذا يجب الإيمان أيضا بما يكون فيه.
Then he began to speak about the time of occurrence of the gathering and the accounting and its terrors, and he said: (And the Last Day) – it is the Day of Resurrection, and what is meant by it is from the time of the gathering to what has no end, or until the people of Paradise enter Paradise and the people of the Fire enter the Fire. It is named thus because it is the last of the limited times, and because there is no night after it, and because it is the last of the days of the world. (Then the horror of the standing) – that is, its enormities and what befalls people therein of hardships and calamities, such as the length of the standing, and the sweat bridling people until it reaches their ears, and it goes into the earth seventy cubits, and the flying of the books to the right hands and the left hands, and their clinging to necks, and the questioning, and the testimony of tongues, hands, feet, hearing, sight, skins, the earth, the night and day, and the noble guardians, and the change of colors. And it is apparent, as al-Sa'd said, that none of what was mentioned befalls the prophets, nor the saints, nor the rest of the righteous; due to His statement, exalted is He: 'The angels descend upon them' [Fussilat 30] – the verse – 'the greatest terror will not grieve them' [al-Anbiya 103]. And the fear of the prophets and angels is a fear of reverence and veneration, even though they are safe from the punishment of Allah. And His statement: (True) – that is, established without fail – is the predicate of 'the Last Day' and what is coordinated with it; so it is obligatory to believe in it due to its coming in the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the Muslims upon it. He said, exalted is He: 'O people, fear your Lord; indeed the earthquake of the Hour is a great thing' [al-Hajj 1] up to His saying: 'but the punishment of Allah is severe' [al-Hajj 2]; and 'Indeed we fear from our Lord a frowning, harsh day' [al-Insan 10]; 'A day that makes the children gray-headed' [al-Muzzammil 17]; 'For every man among them on that day will be a matter that occupies him' [Abasa 37]; 'On the day when faces become white and faces become black' [Aal Imran 106]. And he indicated by his saying: (So lighten, O Merciful) its terrors and enormities, (and assist us) – that is, help us against it… up to [the point] that it varies according to the varying conditions of people: it is intensified upon the disbelievers until they experience the utmost of its length; it is moderate upon the sinful believers; and it is lightened upon the righteous until it is like the prayer of two rak'ahs. Likewise, it is obligatory to believe in whatever occurs therein.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poem line that affirms the Last Day and the horrors of the standing. The commentator first defines the Last Day (al-yawm al-ākhir) as the Day of Resurrection, from the time of the gathering (al-ḥashr) to eternity, or until the final destination of Paradise and Hell. It is called the Last Day because it is the last of the limited times, has no night after it, and is the last of the days of this world. Then he explains 'the horror of the standing' (hawl al-mawqif) as the enormities and afflictions people face on that day: the lengthy standing, sweat that bridles them up to their ears and goes seventy cubits into the earth, the flying of the record books to right and left, the books clinging to necks, questioning, and the testimony of all body parts and even the earth, night and day, and the noble guardian angels, along with changes in color. He cites al-Sa'd's view that none of these horrors afflict the prophets, saints, or righteous believers, supported by Quranic verses that the greatest terror does not grieve them, and that their fear is only of reverence, not of punishment. Then he affirms that the Last Day and its horrors are true (ḥaqq) – established beyond doubt – and belief in them is obligatory due to their confirmation in the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. He provides several Quranic proofs describing the terror of that day. Finally, he explains the supplication 'So lighten, O Merciful, and assist us' as a plea for relief, noting that the intensity of the standing varies: severe for disbelievers, moderate for sinful believers, and light for the righteous (like two rak'ahs of prayer). He concludes that belief is obligatory for all that occurs on that day.

Key Terms
اليوم الآخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is NOT a reason the Day of Resurrection is called the 'Last Day'?
A) It is the last of the limited times.
B) There is no night after it.
C) It is the last of the days of this world.
D) It is the day of the great terror.

Key Takeaway
The Last Day is the eternal day that follows this world, with no night, and belief in it is the foundation of eschatology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Last Day has no night after it—meaning no break, no end. Are you living today as if this world has a final day that will not pause?
?
You know the Last Day is the last of limited times. What limited time in your life are you wasting right now that you will wish you had used for the Hereafter?
?
This day is named the Last because it is the last of worldly days. Have you truly accepted that every worldly day is counting down to that day, or do you live as if this world will never end?

The Horrors of the Standing and the Exemption of the Righteous
Description of horrors and exemption of the righteous

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific horrors of the Day of Judgment and that the prophets, saints, and righteous are exempt from them.

Core Idea
The terrors of that day are immense, but the righteous are spared by Allah's grace.

The commentator now details the terrifying events that occur at the standing—sweat, flying books, testimony of body parts—but immediately clarifies that these do not afflict the prophets and righteous. This balance of fear and hope is central to Islamic eschatology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the list of horrors and then the exception for the righteous.
2
List the specific horrors mentioned
The passage lists: long standing, sweat reaching ears and going deep into earth, flying books to right and left, books on necks, questioning, testimony of body parts, change of colors. Identify each.
There are many; focus on the main ones.
3
Understand the exemption of the righteous
The commentator states that none of these horrors befall prophets, saints, or righteous believers. He supports this with Quranic verses. Reflect on why this is significant.
Their fear is of reverence, not punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you are counted among the righteous who will be spared from the horrors of that day? If not, what is one concrete change you will make today to join their ranks?
?

?

Obligatory Belief in the Last Day and its Variations
Affirmation of truth, Quranic proofs, and variation of intensity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in the Last Day is obligatory, supported by Quran and Sunnah, and that its horrors vary in intensity based on one's faith and deeds.

Core Idea
The reality of the Last Day is certain, and its intensity is proportioned to one's state.

The commentator now affirms that the Last Day and its horrors are an established truth requiring belief. He provides multiple Quranic proofs and explains the supplication for ease. Finally, he describes how the severity of the standing varies for different people, from intense for disbelievers to light for the righteous.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the affirmation of 'truth' and the Quranic verses quoted.
2
Identify the sources of obligation
The commentator says belief is obligatory due to the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. Reflect on what this means for a believer.
These are the three primary sources in Islam.
3
Understand the variation in intensity
The passage concludes by explaining that the horrors vary: severe for disbelievers, moderate for sinful believers, and light for the righteous (like two rak'ahs of prayer).
This shows Allah's justice and mercy.

ثُمَّ شرعَ في الكلام على زمن وقوع الحَشْرِ والحِساب وأهواله فقال: (وَاليَوْمُ الآخِرُ) وهو يوم القيامة، والمراد به من وقت الحشرِ إلى ما لا يتناهى، أو إلى أنْ يَدخُلَ أهلُ الجَنَّةِ الجَنَّةَ وأهلُ النَّارِ النَّارَ، سُمِّي بذلك؛ لأنَّه آخر الأوقات المحدودة، ولأنَّه لا ليلَ بعده، ولأنَّه آخر أيَّام الدنيا. (ثُمَّ هَوْلُ المَوْقِفِ) أي: عظائمه وما يَنَالُ النَّاس فيه مِن الشَّدائد والمصائب، كطُّول الوقوف، وإلجام العَرَق النَّاسَ حتَّى يبلغ آذانهم، ويذهب في الأرض سبعين ذراعاً، وتطاير الكتب بالأيمان والشمائل، ولزومها الأعناق، والمُسَاءَلَةِ، وشهادة الألسنة والأيدي والأرجل والسمع والبصر والجلود والأرض والليل والنهار والحَفَظة الكرام، وتغير الألوان، والظاهر كما قال السعد: أنَّه لا ينال شيءٌ ممَّا ذُكِرَ الأنبياء ولا الأولياء ولا سائر الصُلَحَاءِ؛ لقوله تعالى: تَتَنَزَّلُ عَلَيْهِمُ الْمَلَائِكَةُ [فصلت: 30] الآية لَا يَحْزُنُهُمُ الْفَزَعُ الْأَكْبَرُ [الأنبياء: 103]، وخوفُ الأنبياء والملائكة خَوْفُ إعظام وإجلال، وإن كانوا آمنين عذاب الله، وقوله: (حَقٌّ)، أي: ثابت لا محالة، خبر «اليوم الآخر» وما عُطف عليه؛ فيجب الإيمان به؛ لورُودِه كتاباً وسُنَّةً وإجماع المسلمين عليه، قال تعالى: يَا أَيُّهَا النَّاسُ اتَّقُوا رَبَّكُمْ إِنَّ زَلْزَلَةَ السَّاعَةِ شَيْءٍ عَظِيمُ [الحج: 1] إلى قوله: وَلَكِنَّ عَذَابَ اللَّهِ شَدِيدٌ [الحج: 2]، وإِنَّا نَخَافُ مِن رَّبِّنَا يَوْمًا عَبُوسًا قَمْطَرِيرًا [الإنسان: 10] يَوْمًا يَجْعَلُ الْوِلْدَانَ شِيبًا [المزمل: 17] لِكُلِّ امْرِئٍ مِنْهُمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ شَأْنٌ يُغْنِيهِ [عبس: 37]، يَوْمَ تَبْيَضُ وُجُوهٌ وَتَسْوَدُّ وُجُوهُ [آل عمران: 106]. وأشار بقوله: (فَخَفِّفْ يَا رَحِيمُ) أهواله وعظائِمَه (وَاسْعِفِ)، أي: وأعنا عليه.. إلى أنَّه مُختَلِفٌ باختلاف أحوال النَّاس، فيُشدَّدُ على الكُفَّار حتّى يجدوا من طوله الغاية، ويُتوسّط على فسقة المؤمنين، ويُخَفَّفُ على الصَّالحين حتّى يكون كصلاة ركعتين، وكذا يجب الإيمان أيضا بما يكون فيه.
Then he began to speak about the time of occurrence of the gathering and the accounting and its terrors, and he said: (And the Last Day) – it is the Day of Resurrection, and what is meant by it is from the time of the gathering to what has no end, or until the people of Paradise enter Paradise and the people of the Fire enter the Fire. It is named thus because it is the last of the limited times, and because there is no night after it, and because it is the last of the days of the world. (Then the horror of the standing) – that is, its enormities and what befalls people therein of hardships and calamities, such as the length of the standing, and the sweat bridling people until it reaches their ears, and it goes into the earth seventy cubits, and the flying of the books to the right hands and the left hands, and their clinging to necks, and the questioning, and the testimony of tongues, hands, feet, hearing, sight, skins, the earth, the night and day, and the noble guardians, and the change of colors. And it is apparent, as al-Sa'd said, that none of what was mentioned befalls the prophets, nor the saints, nor the rest of the righteous; due to His statement, exalted is He: 'The angels descend upon them' [Fussilat 30] – the verse – 'the greatest terror will not grieve them' [al-Anbiya 103]. And the fear of the prophets and angels is a fear of reverence and veneration, even though they are safe from the punishment of Allah. And His statement: (True) – that is, established without fail – is the predicate of 'the Last Day' and what is coordinated with it; so it is obligatory to believe in it due to its coming in the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus of the Muslims upon it. He said, exalted is He: 'O people, fear your Lord; indeed the earthquake of the Hour is a great thing' [al-Hajj 1] up to His saying: 'but the punishment of Allah is severe' [al-Hajj 2]; and 'Indeed we fear from our Lord a frowning, harsh day' [al-Insan 10]; 'A day that makes the children gray-headed' [al-Muzzammil 17]; 'For every man among them on that day will be a matter that occupies him' [Abasa 37]; 'On the day when faces become white and faces become black' [Aal Imran 106]. And he indicated by his saying: (So lighten, O Merciful) its terrors and enormities, (and assist us) – that is, help us against it… up to [the point] that it varies according to the varying conditions of people: it is intensified upon the disbelievers until they experience the utmost of its length; it is moderate upon the sinful believers; and it is lightened upon the righteous until it is like the prayer of two rak'ahs. Likewise, it is obligatory to believe in whatever occurs therein.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poem line that affirms the Last Day and the horrors of the standing. The commentator first defines the Last Day (al-yawm al-ākhir) as the Day of Resurrection, from the time of the gathering (al-ḥashr) to eternity, or until the final destination of Paradise and Hell. It is called the Last Day because it is the last of the limited times, has no night after it, and is the last of the days of this world. Then he explains 'the horror of the standing' (hawl al-mawqif) as the enormities and afflictions people face on that day: the lengthy standing, sweat that bridles them up to their ears and goes seventy cubits into the earth, the flying of the record books to right and left, the books clinging to necks, questioning, and the testimony of all body parts and even the earth, night and day, and the noble guardian angels, along with changes in color. He cites al-Sa'd's view that none of these horrors afflict the prophets, saints, or righteous believers, supported by Quranic verses that the greatest terror does not grieve them, and that their fear is only of reverence, not of punishment. Then he affirms that the Last Day and its horrors are true (ḥaqq) – established beyond doubt – and belief in them is obligatory due to their confirmation in the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. He provides several Quranic proofs describing the terror of that day. Finally, he explains the supplication 'So lighten, O Merciful, and assist us' as a plea for relief, noting that the intensity of the standing varies: severe for disbelievers, moderate for sinful believers, and light for the righteous (like two rak'ahs of prayer). He concludes that belief is obligatory for all that occurs on that day.

Key Terms
حَقّ
زَلْزَلَة الساعة
فزع أكبر

A) Severe and unbearable
B) Moderate
C) Light as two rak'ahs of prayer
D) Nonexistent

Key Takeaway
Belief in the Last Day is obligatory, and its horrors are justly varied according to one's state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you contemplate the varying intensities of the Last Day, which category do you truly believe you belong to? What in your daily life confirms or contradicts that belief?
?
The passage quotes the supplication 'Lighten, O Merciful, its terrors and assist us against it.' When was the last time you made such a needy plea about the Day of Resurrection, and what does your lack of it reveal about the reality of your faith?
?
If the Day is as certain as the claim (ḥaqq) with Quranic proof, why do your choices today—your time, your worries, your priorities—often ignore its weight? What one concrete change will you make to live in its shadow?
